Title:  dollars and I don't want no di'monds."
Title:  
Title:     "All right. But I bet you I ain't going to throw off on di'monds. Some of
Title:  'em's worth twenty dollars apiece -- there ain't any, hardly, but's worth
Title:  six bits or a dollar."
Title:  
Title:     "No! Is that so?"
Title:  
Title:     "Cert'nly -- anybody'll tell you so. Hain't you ever seen one, Huck?"
Title:  
Title:     "Not as I remember."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, kings have slathers of them."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I don' know no kings, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon you don't. But if you was to go to Europe you'd see a raft of
Title:  'em hopping around."
Title:  
Title:     "Do they hop?"
Title:  
Title:     "Hop? -- your granny! No!"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, what did you say they did, for?"
Title:  
Title:     "Shucks, I only meant you'd see 'em -- not hopping, of course -- what do
Title:  they want to hop for? -- but I mean you'd just see 'em -- scattered around,
Title:  you know, in a kind of a general way. Like that old humpbacked Richard."
Title:  
Title:     "Richard? What's his other name?"
Title:  
Title:     "He didn't have any other name. Kings don't have any but a given name."
Title:  
Title:     "No?"
Title:  
Title:     "But they don't."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, if they like it, Tom, all right; but I don't want to be a king and
Title:  have only just a given name, like a nigger. But say -- where you going to
Title:  dig first?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I don't know. S'pose we tackle that old dead-limb tree on the hill
Title:  t'other side of Still-House branch?"
Title:  
Title:     "I'm agreed."
Title:  
Title:     So they got a crippled pick and a shovel, and set out on their three-mile
Title:  tramp. They arrived hot and panting, and threw themselves down in the shade
Title:  of a neighboring elm to rest and have a smoke.
Title:  
Title:     "I like this," said Tom.
Title:  
Title:     "So do I."
Title:  
Title:     "Say, Huck, if we find a treasure here, what you going to do with your
Title:  share?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I'll have pie and a glass of soda every day, and I'll go to every
Title:  circus that comes along. I bet I'll have a gay time."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, ain't you going to save any of it?"
Title:  
Title:     "Save it? What for?"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, so as to have something to live on, by and by."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, that ain't any use. Pap would come back to thish-yer town some day
Title:  and get his claws on it
Title:  if I didn't hurry up, and I tell you he'd clean it out pretty quick. What
Title:  you going to do with yourn, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "I'm going to buy a new drum, and a sure-'nough sword, and a red necktie
Title:  and a bull pup, and get married."
Title:  
Title:     "Married!"
Title:  
Title:     "That's it."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, you -- why, you ain't in your right mind."
Title:  
Title:     "Wait -- you'll see."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's the foolishest thing you could do. Look at pap and my
Title:  mother. Fight! Why, they used to fight all the time. I remember, mighty
Title:  well."
Title:  
Title:     "That ain't anything. The girl I'm going to marry won't fight."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, I reckon they're all alike. They'll all comb a body. Now you better
Title:  think 'bout this awhile. I tell you you better. What's the name of the gal?"
Title:  
Title:     "It ain't a gal at all -- it's a girl."
Title:  
Title:     "It's all the same, I reckon; some says gal, some says girl -- both's
Title:  right, like enough. Anyway, what's her name, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "I'll tell you some time -- not now."
Title:  
Title:     "All right -- that'll do. Only if you get married I'll be more lonesomer
Title:  than ever."
Title:  
Title:     "No you won't. You'll come and live with me. Now stir out of this and
Title:  we'll go to digging."
Title:  
Title:     They worked and sweated for half an hour. No
Title:  result. They toiled another half-hour. Still no result. Huck said:
Title:  
Title:     "Do they always bury it as deep as this?"
Title:  
Title:     "Sometimes -- not always. Not generally. I reckon we haven't got the
Title:  right place."
Title:  
Title:     So they chose a new spot and began again. The labor dragged a little, but
Title:  still they made progress. They pegged away in silence for some time. Finally
Title:  Huck leaned on his shovel, swabbed the beaded drops from his brow with his
Title:  sleeve, and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Where you going to dig next, after we get this one?"
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon maybe we'll tackle the old tree that's over yonder on Cardiff
Title:  Hill back of the widow's."
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon that'll be a good one. But won't the widow take it away from
Title:  us, Tom? It's on her land."
Title:  
Title:     "She take it away! Maybe she'd like to try it once. Whoever finds one of
Title:  these hid treasures, it belongs to him. It don't make any difference whose
Title:  land it's on."
Title:  
Title:     That was satisfactory. The work went on. By and by Huck said:
Title:  
Title:     "Blame it, we must be in the wrong place again. What do you think?"
Title:  
Title:     "It is mighty curious, Huck. I don't understand it. Sometimes witches
Title:  interfere. I reckon maybe that's what's the trouble now."
Title:  
Title:     "Shucks! Witches ain't got no power in the daytime."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's so. I didn't think of that. Oh, I know what the matter is!
Title:  What a blamed lot of fools we are! You got to find out where the shadow of
Title:  the limb falls at midnight, and that's where you dig!"
Title:  
Title:     "Then consound it, we've fooled away all this work for nothing. Now hang
Title:  it all, we got to come back in the night. It's an awful long way. Can you
Title:  get out?"
Title:  
Title:     "I bet I will. We've got to do it to-night, too, because if somebody sees
Title:  these holes they'll know in a minute what's here and they'll go for it."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I'll come around and maow to-night."
Title:  
Title:     "All right. Let's hide the tools in the bushes."
Title:  
Title:     The boys were there that night, about the appointed time. They sat in the
Title:  shadow waiting. It was a lonely place, and an hour made solemn by old
Title:  traditions. Spirits whispered in the rustling leaves, ghosts lurked in the
Title:  murky nooks, the deep baying of a hound floated up out of the distance, an
Title:  owl answered with his sepulchral note. The boys were subdued by these
Title:  solemnities, and talked little. By and by they judged that twelve had come;
Title:  they marked where the shadow fell, and began to dig. Their hopes commenced
Title:  to rise. Their interest grew stronger, and their industry kept pace with it.
Title:  The hole deepened and still deepened, but every time their hearts jumped to
Title:  hear the pick strike upon something, they only suffered a new
Title:  disappointment. It was only a stone or a chunk. At last Tom said:
Title:     "It ain't any use, Huck, we're wrong again."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, but we can't be wrong. We spotted the shadder to a dot."
Title:  
Title:     "I know it, but then there's another thing."
Title:  
Title:     "What's that?".
Title:  
Title:     "Why, we only guessed at the time. Like enough it was too late or too
Title:  early."
Title:  
Title:     Huck dropped his shovel.
Title:  
Title:     "That's it," said he. "That's the very trouble. We got to give this one
Title:  up. We can't ever tell the right time, and besides this kind of thing's too
Title:  awful, here this time of night with witches and ghosts a-fluttering around
Title:  so. I feel as if something's behind me all the time; and I'm afeard to turn
Title:  around, becuz maybe there's others in front a-waiting for a chance. I been
Title:  creeping all over, ever since I got here."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I've been pretty much so, too, Huck. They most always put in a
Title:  dead man when they bury a treasure under a tree, to look out for it."
Title:  
Title:     "Lordy!"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, they do. I've always heard that."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, I don't like to fool around much where there's dead people. A
Title:  body's bound to get into trouble with 'em, sure."
Title:  
Title:     "I don't like to stir 'em up, either. S'pose this one here was to stick
Title:  his skull out and say something!"
Title:  
Title:     "Don't Tom! It's awful."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, it just is. Huck, I don't feel comfortable a bit."
Title:  
Title:     "Say, Tom, let's give this place up, and try somewheres else."
Title:  
Title:     "All right, I reckon we better."
Title:  
Title:     "What'll it be?"
Title:  
Title:     Tom considered awhile; and then said:
Title:  
Title:     "The ha'nted house. That's it!"
Title:  
Title:     "Blame it, I don't like ha'nted houses, Tom. Why, they're a dern sight
Title:  worse'n dead people. Dead people might talk, maybe, but they don't come
Title:  sliding around in a shroud, when you ain't noticing, and peep over your
Title:  shoulder all of a sudden and grit their teeth, the way a ghost does. I
Title:  couldn't stand such a thing as that, Tom -- nobody could."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, but, Huck, ghosts don't travel around only at night. They won't
Title:  hender us from digging there in the daytime."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's so. But you know mighty well people don't go about that
Title:  ha'nted house in the day nor the night."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's mostly because they don't like to go where a man's been
Title:  murdered, anyway -- but nothing's ever been seen around that house except in
Title:  the night -- just some blue lights slipping by the windows -- no regular
Title:  ghosts."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, where you see one of them blue lights flickering around, Tom, you
Title:  can bet there's a ghost mighty close behind it. It stands to reason. Becuz
Title:  you know that they don't anybody but ghosts use 'em."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, that's so. But anyway they don't come around in the daytime, so
Title:  what's the use of our being afeard?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, all right. We'll tackle the ha'nted house if you say so -- but I
Title:  reckon it's taking chances."
Title:  
Title:     They had started down the hill by this time. There in the middle of the
Title:  moonlit valley below them stood the "ha'nted" house, utterly isolated, its
Title:  fences gone long ago, rank weeds smothering the very doorsteps, the chimney
Title:  crumbled to ruin, the window-sashes vacant, a corner of the roof caved in.
Title:  The boys gazed awhile, half expecting to see a blue light flit past a
Title:  window; then talking in a low tone, as befitted the time and the
Title:  circumstances, they struck far off to the right, to give the haunted house a
Title:  wide berth, and took their way homeward through the woods that adorned the
Title:  rearward side of Cardiff Hill.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXVI
Title:  
Title:     ABOUT noon the next day the boys arrived at the dead tree; they had come
Title:  for their tools. Tom was impatient to go to the haunted house; Huck was
Title:  measurably so, also -- but suddenly said:
Title:  
Title:     "Lookyhere, Tom, do you know what day it is?"
Title:  
Title:     Tom mentally ran over the days of the week, and then quickly lifted his
Title:  eyes with a startled look in them --
Title:  
Title:     "My! I never once thought of it, Huck!"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I didn't neither, but all at once it popped onto me that it was
Title:  Friday."
Title:  
Title:     "Blame it, a body can't be too careful, Huck. We might 'a' got into an
Title:  awful scrape, tackling such a thing on a Friday."
Title:  
Title:     "Might ! Better say we would! There's some lucky days, maybe, but Friday
Title:  ain't."
Title:  
Title:     "Any fool knows that. I don't reckon you was the first that found it out,
Title:  Huck."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I never said I was, did I? And Friday ain't all, neither. I had a
Title:  rotten bad dream last night -- dreampt about rats."
Title:  
Title:     "No! Sure sign of trouble. Did they fight?"
Title:  
Title:     "No."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's good, Huck. When they don't fight it's only a sign that
Title:  there's trouble around, you know. All we got to do is to look mighty sharp
Title:  and keep out of it. We'll drop this thing for to-day, and play. Do you know
Title:  Robin Hood, Huck?"
Title:  
Title:     "No. Who's Robin Hood?"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, he was one of the greatest men that was ever in England -- and the
Title:  best. He was a robber."
Title:  
Title:     "Cracky, I wisht I was. Who did he rob?"
Title:  
Title:     "Only sheriffs and bishops and rich people and kings, and such like. But
Title:  he never bothered the poor. He loved 'em. He always divided up with 'em
Title:  perfectly square."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, he must 'a' been a brick."
Title:  
Title:     "I bet you he was, Huck. Oh, he was the noblest man that ever was. They
Title:  ain't any such men now, I can tell you. He could lick any man in England,
Title:  with one hand tied behind him; and he could take his yew bow and plug a
Title:  ten-cent piece every time, a mile and a half."
Title:  
Title:     "What's a yew bow?"
Title:  
Title:     "I don't know. It's some kind of a bow, of course. And if he hit that
Title:  dime only on the edge he would set down and cry -- and curse. But we'll play
Title:  Robin Hood -- it's nobby fun. I'll learn you."
Title:  
Title:     "I'm agreed."
Title:  
Title:     So they played Robin Hood all the afternoon, now and then casting a
Title:  yearning eye down upon the haunted house and passing a remark about the
Title:  morrow's prospects and possibilities there. As the sun began to sink into
Title:  the west they took their way homeward athwart the long shadows of the trees
Title:  and soon were buried from sight in the forests of Cardiff Hill.
Title:  
Title:     On Saturday, shortly after noon, the boys were at the dead tree again.
Title:  They had a smoke and a chat in the shade, and then dug a little in their
Title:  last hole, not with great hope, but merely because Tom said there were so
Title:  many cases where people had given up a treasure after getting down within
Title:  six inches of it, and then somebody else had come along and turned it up
Title:  with a single thrust of a shovel. The thing failed this time, however, so
Title:  the boys shouldered their tools and went away feeling that they had not
Title:  trifled with fortune, but had fulfilled all the requirements that belong to
Title:  the business of treasure-hunting.
Title:  
Title:     When they reached the haunted house there was something so weird and
Title:  grisly about the dead silence that reigned there under the baking sun, and
Title:  something so depressing about the loneliness and desolation of the place,
Title:  that they were afraid, for a moment, to venture in. Then they crept to the
Title:  door and took a trembling peep. They saw a weed-grown, floorless room,
Title:  unplastered, an ancient fireplace, vacant windows, a ruinous staircase; and
Title:  here, there, and everywhere hung ragged and abandoned cobwebs. They
Title:  presently entered, softly, with quickened pulses, talking in whispers, ears
Title:  alert to catch the slightest sound, and muscles tense and ready for instant
Title:  retreat.
Title:  
Title:     In a little while familiarity modified their fears and they gave the
Title:  place a critical and interested examination, rather admiring their own
Title:  boldness, and wondering at it, too. Next they wanted to look up-stairs. This
Title:  was something like cutting off retreat, but they got to daring each other,
Title:  and of course there could be but one result -- they threw their tools into a
Title:  corner and made the ascent. Up there were the same signs of decay. In one
Title:  corner they found a closet that promised mystery, but the promise was a
Title:  fraud -- there was nothing in it. Their courage was up now and well in hand.
Title:  They were about to go down and begin work when --
Title:  
Title:     "Sh!" said Tom.
Title:  
Title:     "What is it?" whispered Huck, blanching with fright.
Title:  
Title:     "Sh! ... There! ... Hear it?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes! ... Oh, my! Let's run!"
Title:  
Title:     "Keep still! Don't you budge! They're coming right toward the door."
Title:  
Title:     The boys stretched themselves upon the floor with their eyes to
Title:  knot-holes in the planking, and lay waiting, in a misery of fear.
Title:  
Title:     "They've stopped.... No -- coming.... Here they are. Don't whisper
Title:  another word, Huck. My goodness, I wish I was out of this!"
Title:  
Title:     Two men entered. Each boy said to himself: "There's the old deaf and dumb
Title:  Spaniard that's
Title:  been about town once or twice lately -- never saw t'other man before."
Title:  
Title:     "T'other" was a ragged, unkempt creature, with nothing very pleasant in
Title:  his face. The Spaniard was wrapped in a serape; he had bushy white whiskers;
Title:  long white hair flowed from under his sombrero, and he wore green goggles.
Title:  When they came in, "t'other" was talking in a low voice; they sat down on
Title:  the ground, facing the door, with their backs to the wall, and the speaker
Title:  continued his remarks. His manner became less guarded and his words more
Title:  distinct as he proceeded:
Title:  
Title:     "No," said he, "I've thought it all over, and I don't like it. It's
Title:  dangerous."
Title:  
Title:     "Dangerous!" grunted the "deaf and dumb" Spaniard -- to the vast surprise
Title:  of the boys. "Milksop!"
Title:  
Title:     This voice made the boys gasp and quake. It was Injun Joe's! There was
Title:  silence for some time. Then Joe said:
Title:  
Title:     "What's any more dangerous than that job up yonder -- but nothing's come
Title:  of it."
Title:  
Title:     "That's different. Away up the river so, and not another house about.
Title:  'Twon't ever be known that we tried, anyway, long as we didn't succeed."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, what's more dangerous than coming here in the daytime! -- anybody
Title:  would suspicion us that saw us."
Title:  
Title:     "I know that. But there warn't any other place as handy after that fool
Title:  of a job. I want to quit
Title:  this shanty. I wanted to yesterday, only it warn't any use trying to stir
Title:  out of here, with those infernal boys playing over there on the hill right
Title:  in full view."
Title:  
Title:     "Those infernal boys" quaked again under the inspiration of this remark,
Title:  and thought how lucky it was that they had remembered it was Friday and
Title:  concluded to wait a day. They wished in their hearts they had waited a year.
Title:  
Title:     The two men got out some food and made a luncheon. After a long and
Title:  thoughtful silence, Injun Joe said:
Title:  
Title:     "Look here, lad -- you go back up the river where you belong. Wait there
Title:  till you hear from me. I'll take the chances on dropping into this town just
Title:  once more, for a look. We'll do that 'dangerous' job after I've spied around
Title:  a little and think things look well for it. Then for Texas! We'll leg it
Title:  together!"
Title:  
Title:     This was satisfactory. Both men presently fell to yawning, and Injun Joe
Title:  said:
Title:  
Title:     "I'm dead for sleep! It's your turn to watch."
Title:  
Title:     He curled down in the weeds and soon began to snore. His comrade stirred
Title:  him once or twice and he became quiet. Presently the watcher began to nod;
Title:  his head drooped lower and lower, both men began to snore now.
Title:  
Title:     The boys drew a long, grateful breath. Tom whispered:
Title:  
Title:     "Now's our chance -- come!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck said:
Title:  
Title:     "I can't -- I'd die if they was to wake."
Title:  
Title:     Tom urged -- Huck held back. At last Tom rose slowly and softly, and
Title:  started alone. But the first step he made wrung such a hideous creak from
Title:  the crazy floor that he sank down almost dead with fright. He never made a
Title:  second attempt. The boys lay there counting the dragging moments till it
Title:  seemed to them that time must be done and eternity growing gray; and then
Title:  they were grateful to note that at last the sun was setting.
Title:  
Title:     Now one snore ceased. Injun Joe sat up, stared around -- smiled grimly
Title:  upon his comrade, whose head was drooping upon his knees -- stirred him up
Title:  with his foot and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Here! You're a watchman, ain't you! All right, though -- nothing's
Title:  happened."
Title:  
Title:     "My! have I been asleep?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, partly, partly. Nearly time for us to be moving, pard. What'll we do
Title:  with what little swag we've got left?"
Title:  
Title:     "I don't know -- leave it here as we've always done, I reckon. No use to
Title:  take it away till we start south. Six hundred and fifty in silver's
Title:  something to carry."
Title:  
Title:     "Well -- all right -- it won't matter to come here once more."
Title:  
Title:     "No -- but I'd say come in the night as we used to do -- it's better."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes: but look here; it may be a good while
Title:  before I get the right chance at that job; accidents might happen; 'tain't
Title:  in such a very good place; we'll just regularly bury it -- and bury it
Title:  deep."
Title:  
Title:     "Good idea," said the comrade, who walked across the room, knelt down,
Title:  raised one of the rearward hearth-stones and took out a bag that jingled
Title:  pleasantly. He subtracted from it twenty or thirty dollars for himself and
Title:  as much for Injun Joe, and passed the bag to the latter, who was on his
Title:  knees in the corner, now, digging with his bowie-knife.
Title:  
Title:     The boys forgot all their fears, all their miseries in an instant. With
Title:  gloating eyes they watched every movement. Luck! -- the splendor of it was
Title:  beyond all imagination! Six hundred dollars was money enough to make half a
Title:  dozen boys rich! Here was treasure hunting under the happiest auspices --
Title:  there would not be any bothersome uncertainty as to where to dig. They
Title:  nudged each other every moment -- eloquent nudges and easily understood, for
Title:  they simply meant -- "Oh, but ain't you glad now we're here!"
Title:  
Title:     Joe's knife struck upon something.
Title:  
Title:     "Hello!" said he.
Title:  
Title:     "What is it?" said his comrade.
Title:  
Title:     "Half-rotten plank -- no, it's a box, I believe. Here -- bear a hand and
Title:  we'll see what it's here for. Never mind, I've broke a hole."
Title:  
Title:     He reached his hand in and drew it out --
Title:  
Title:     "Man, it's money!"
Title:  
Title:     The two men examined the handful of coins. They were gold. The boys above
Title:  were as excited as themselves, and as delighted.
Title:  
Title:     Joe's comrade said:
Title:  
Title:     "We'll make quick work of this. There's an old rusty pick over amongst
Title:  the weeds in the corner the other side of the fireplace -- I saw it a minute
Title:  ago."
Title:  
Title:     He ran and brought the boys' pick and shovel. Injun Joe took the pick,
Title:  looked it over critically, shook his head, muttered something to himself,
Title:  and then began to use it. The box was soon unearthed. It was not very large;
Title:  it was iron bound and had been very strong before the slow years had injured
Title:  it. The men contemplated the treasure awhile in blissful silence.
Title:  
Title:     "Pard, there's thousands of dollars here," said Injun Joe.
Title:  
Title:     "'Twas always said that Murrel's gang used to be around here one summer,"
Title:  the stranger observed.
Title:  
Title:     "I know it," said Injun Joe; "and this looks like it, I should say."
Title:  
Title:     "Now you won't need to do that job."
Title:  
Title:     The half-breed frowned. Said he:
Title:  
Title:     "You don't know me. Least you don't know all about that thing. 'Tain't
Title:  robbery altogether -- it's revenge!" and a wicked light flamed in his eyes.
Title:  "I'll need your help in it. When it's finished -- then Texas. Go home to
Title:  your Nance and your kids, and stand by till you hear from me."
Title:  
Title:     "Well -- if you say so; what'll we do with this -- bury it again?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes. [Ravishing delight overhead.] No ! by the great Sachem, no!
Title:  [Profound distress overhead.] I'd nearly forgot. That pick had fresh earth
Title:  on it! [The boys were sick with terror in a moment.] What business has a
Title:  pick and a shovel here? What business with fresh earth on them? Who brought
Title:  them here -- and where are they gone? Have you heard anybody? -- seen
Title:  anybody? What! bury it again and leave them to come and see the ground
Title:  disturbed? Not exactly -- not exactly. We'll take it to my den."
Title:  
Title:     "Why, of course! Might have thought of that before. You mean Number One?"
Title:  
Title:     "No -- Number Two -- under the cross. The other place is bad -- too
Title:  common."
Title:  
Title:     "All right. It's nearly dark enough to start."
Title:  
Title:     Injun Joe got up and went about from window to window cautiously peeping
Title:  out. Presently he said:
Title:  
Title:     "Who could have brought those tools here? Do you reckon they can be
Title:  up-stairs?"
Title:  
Title:     The boys' breath forsook them. Injun Joe put his hand on his knife,
Title:  halted a moment, undecided, and then turned toward the stairway. The boys
Title:  thought of the closet, but their strength was gone. The steps came creaking
Title:  up the stairs -- the intolerable distress of the situation woke the stricken
Title:  resolution of the lads -- they were about to spring for the closet, when
Title:  there was a crash of rotten timbers
Title:  and Injun Joe landed on the ground amid the debris of the ruined stairway.
Title:  He gathered himself up cursing, and his comrade said:
Title:  
Title:     "Now what's the use of all that? If it's anybody, and they're up there,
Title:  let them stay there -- who cares? If they want to jump down, now, and get
Title:  into trouble, who objects? It will be dark in fifteen minutes -- and then
Title:  let them follow us if they want to. I'm willing. In my opinion, whoever hove
Title:  those things in here caught a sight of us and took us for ghosts or devils
Title:  or something. I'll bet they're running yet."
Title:  
Title:     Joe grumbled awhile; then he agreed with his friend that what daylight
Title:  was left ought to be economized in getting things ready for leaving. Shortly
Title:  afterward they slipped out of the house in the deepening twilight, and moved
Title:  toward the river with their precious box.
Title:  
Title:     Tom and Huck rose up, weak but vastly relieved, and stared after them
Title:  through the chinks between the logs of the house. Follow? Not they. They
Title:  were content to reach ground again without broken necks, and take the
Title:  townward track over the hill. They did not talk much. They were too much
Title:  absorbed in hating themselves -- hating the ill luck that made them take the
Title:  spade and the pick there. But for that, Injun Joe never would have
Title:  suspected. He would have hidden the silver with the gold to wait there till
Title:  his "revenge" was satisfied, and then he would have had the misfortune to
Title:  find that money turn up missing. Bitter, bitter luck that the tools were
Title:  ever brought there!
Title:  
Title:     They resolved to keep a lookout for that Spaniard when he should come to
Title:  town spying out for chances to do his revengeful job, and follow him to
Title:  "Number Two," wherever that might be. Then a ghastly thought occurred to
Title:  Tom.
Title:  
Title:     "Revenge? What if he means us, Huck!"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, don't!" said Huck, nearly fainting.
Title:  
Title:     They talked it all over, and as they entered town they agreed to believe
Title:  that he might possibly mean somebody else -- at least that he might at least
Title:  mean nobody but Tom, since only Tom had testified.
Title:  
Title:     Very, very small comfort it was to Tom to be alone in danger! Company
Title:  would be a palpable improvement, he thought.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXVII
Title:  
Title:     THE adventure of the day mightily tormented Tom's dreams that night. Four
Title:  times he had his hands on that rich treasure and four times it wasted to
Title:  nothingness in his fingers as sleep forsook him and wakefulness brought back
Title:  the hard reality of his misfortune. As he lay in the early morning recalling
Title:  the incidents of his great adventure, he noticed that they seemed curiously
Title:  subdued and far away -- somewhat as if they had happened in another world,
Title:  or in a time long gone by. Then it occurred to him that the great adventure
Title:  itself must be a dream! There was one very strong argument in favor of this
Title:  idea -- namely, that the quantity of coin he had seen was too vast to be
Title:  real. He had never seen as much as fifty dollars in one mass before, and he
Title:  was like all boys of his age and station in life, in that he imagined that
Title:  all references to "hundreds" and "thousands" were mere fanciful forms of
Title:  speech, and that no such sums really existed in the world. He never had
Title:  supposed for a moment that so large a sum as a hundred dollars was to be
Title:  found in actual money in any one's possession. If his notions of hidden
Title:  treasure had been
Title:  analyzed, they would have been found to consist of a handful of real dimes
Title:  and a bushel of vague, splendid, ungraspable dollars.
Title:  
Title:     But the incidents of his adventure grew sensibly sharper and clearer
Title:  under the attrition of thinking them over, and so he presently found himself
Title:  leaning to the impression that the thing might not have been a dream, after
Title:  all. This uncertainty must be swept away. He would snatch a hurried
Title:  breakfast and go and find Huck. Huck was sitting on the gunwale of a
Title:  flatboat, listlessly dangling his feet in the water and looking very
Title:  melancholy. Tom concluded to let Huck lead up to the subject. If he did not
Title:  do it, then the adventure would be proved to have been only a dream.
Title:  
Title:     "Hello, Huck!"
Title:  
Title:     "Hello, yourself."
Title:  
Title:     Silence, for a minute.
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, if we'd 'a' left the blame tools at the dead tree, we'd 'a' got the
Title:  money. Oh, ain't it awful!"
Title:  
Title:     "'Tain't a dream, then, 'tain't a dream! Somehow I most wish it was.
Title:  Dog'd if I don't, Huck."
Title:  
Title:     "What ain't a dream?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, that thing yesterday. I been half thinking it was."
Title:  
Title:     "Dream! If them stairs hadn't broke down you'd 'a' seen how much dream it
Title:  was! I've had dreams enough all night -- with that patch-eyed Spanish devil
Title:  going for me all through 'em -- rot him!"
Title:  
Title:     "No, not rot him. Find him! Track the money!"
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, we'll never find him. A feller don't have only one chance for such
Title:  a pile -- and that one's lost. I'd feel mighty shaky if I was to see him,
Title:  anyway."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, so'd I; but I'd like to see him, anyway -- and track him out -- to
Title:  his Number Two."
Title:  
Title:     "Number Two -- yes, that's it. I been thinking 'bout that. But I can't
Title:  make nothing out of it. What do you reckon it is?"
Title:  
Title:     "I dono. It's too deep. Say, Huck -- maybe it's the number of a house!"
Title:  
Title:     "Goody! ... No, Tom, that ain't it. If it is, it ain't in this one-horse
Title:  town. They ain't no numbers here."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, that's so. Lemme think a minute. Here -- it's the number of a room
Title:  -- in a tavern, you know!"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, that's the trick! They ain't only two taverns. We can find out
Title:  quick."
Title:  
Title:     "You stay here, Huck, till I come."
Title:  
Title:     Tom was off at once. He did not care to have Huck's company in public
Title:  places. He was gone half an hour. He found that in the best tavern, No. 2
Title:  had long been occupied by a young lawyer, and was still so occupied. In the
Title:  less ostentatious house, No. 2 was a mystery. The tavern-keeper's young son
Title:  said it was kept locked all the time, and he never saw anybody go into it or
Title:  come out of it
Title:  except at night; he did not know any particular reason for this state of
Title:  things; had had some little curiosity, but it was rather feeble; had made
Title:  the most of the mystery by entertaining himself with the idea that that room
Title:  was "ha'nted"; had noticed that there was a light in there the night before.
Title:  
Title:     "That's what I've found out, Huck. I reckon that's the very No. 2 we're
Title:  after."
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon it is, Tom. Now what you going to do?"
Title:  
Title:     "Lemme think."
Title:  
Title:     Tom thought a long time. Then he said:
Title:  
Title:     "I'll tell you. The back door of that No. 2 is the door that comes out
Title:  into that little close alley between the tavern and the old rattle trap of a
Title:  brick store. Now you get hold of all the door-keys you can find, and I'll
Title:  nip all of auntie's, and the first dark night we'll go there and try 'em.
Title:  And mind you, keep a lookout for Injun Joe, because he said he was going to
Title:  drop into town and spy around once more for a chance to get his revenge. If
Title:  you see him, you just follow him; and if he don't go to that No. 2, that
Title:  ain't the place."
Title:  
Title:     "Lordy, I don't want to foller him by myself!"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, it'll be night, sure. He mightn't ever see you -- and if he did,
Title:  maybe he'd never think anything."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, if it's pretty dark I reckon I'll track him. I dono -- I dono.
Title:  I'll try."
Title:  
Title:     "You bet I'll follow him, if it's dark, Huck.
Title:  Why, he might 'a' found out he couldn't get his revenge, and be going right
Title:  after that money."
Title:  
Title:     "It's so, Tom, it's so. I'll foller him; I will, by jingoes!"
Title:  
Title:     "Now you're talking! Don't you ever weaken, Huck, and I won't."
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                 Chapter XXVIII
Title:  
Title:     THAT night Tom and Huck were ready for their adventure. They hung about
Title:  the neighborhood of the tavern until after nine, one watching the alley at a
Title:  distance and the other the tavern door. Nobody entered the alley or left it;
Title:  nobody resembling the Spaniard entered or left the tavern door. The night
Title:  promised to be a fair one; so Tom went home with the understanding that if a
Title:  considerable degree of darkness came on, Huck was to come and "maow,"
Title:  whereupon he would slip out and try the keys. But the night remained clear,
Title:  and Huck closed his watch and retired to bed in an empty sugar hogshead
Title:  about twelve.
Title:  
Title:     Tuesday the boys had the same ill luck. Also Wednesday. But Thursday
Title:  night promised better. Tom slipped out in good season with his aunt's old
Title:  tin lantern, and a large towel to blindfold it with. He hid the lantern in
Title:  Huck's sugar hogshead and the watch began. An hour before midnight the
Title:  tavern closed up and its lights (the only ones thereabouts) were put out. No
Title:  Spaniard had been seen. Nobody had entered or left the alley. Everything
Title:  was auspicious. The blackness of darkness reigned, the perfect stillness was
Title:  interrupted only by occasional mutterings of distant thunder.
Title:  
Title:     Tom got his lantern, lit it in the hogshead, wrapped it closely in the
Title:  towel, and the two adventurers crept in the gloom toward the tavern. Huck
Title:  stood sentry and Tom felt his way into the alley. Then there was a season of
Title:  waiting anxiety that weighed upon Huck's spirits like a mountain. He began
Title:  to wish he could see a flash from the lantern -- it would frighten him, but
Title:  it would at least tell him that Tom was alive yet. It seemed hours since Tom
Title:  had disappeared. Surely he must have fainted; maybe he was dead; maybe his
Title:  heart had burst under terror and excitement. In his uneasiness Huck found
Title:  himself drawing closer and closer to the alley; fearing all sorts of
Title:  dreadful things, and momentarily expecting some catastrophe to happen that
Title:  would take away his breath. There was not much to take away, for he seemed
Title:  only able to inhale it by thimblefuls, and his heart would soon wear itself
Title:  out, the way it was beating. Suddenly there was a flash of light and Tom
Title:  came tearing by him: . "Run!" said he; "run, for your life!"
Title:  
Title:     He needn't have repeated it; once was enough; Huck was making thirty or
Title:  forty miles an hour before the repetition was uttered. The boys never
Title:  stopped till they reached the shed of a deserted slaughter-house at the
Title:  lower end of the village. Just
Title:  as they got within its shelter the storm burst and the rain poured down. As
Title:  soon as Tom got his breath he said:
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, it was awful! I tried two of the keys, just as soft as I could;
Title:  but they seemed to make such a power of racket that I couldn't hardly get my
Title:  breath I was so scared. They wouldn't turn in the lock, either. Well,
Title:  without noticing what I was doing, I took hold of the knob, and open comes
Title:  the door! It warn't locked! I hopped in, and shook off the towel, and, great
Title:  Caesar's ghost!"
Title:  
Title:     "What! -- what'd you see, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, I most stepped onto Injun Joe's hand!"
Title:  
Title:     "No!"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes! He was lying there, sound asleep on the floor, with his old patch
Title:  on his eye and his arms spread out."
Title:  
Title:     "Lordy, what did you do? Did he wake up?"
Title:  
Title:     "No, never budged. Drunk, I reckon. I just grabbed that towel and
Title:  started!"
Title:  
Title:     "I'd never 'a' thought of the towel, I bet!"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I would. My aunt would make me mighty sick if I lost it."
Title:  
Title:     "Say, Tom, did you see that box?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, I didn't wait to look around. I didn't see the box, I didn't see
Title:  the cross. I didn't see anything but a bottle and a tin cup on the floor by
Title:  Injun Joe; yes, I saw two barrels and lots more bottles in the room. Don't
Title:  you see, now, what's the matter with that ha'nted room?"
Title:  
Title:     "How?"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, it's ha'nted with whiskey! Maybe all the Temperance Taverns have
Title:  got a ha'nted room, hey, Huck?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I reckon maybe that's so. Who'd 'a' thought such a thing? But say,
Title:  Tom, now's a mighty good time to get that box, if Injun Joe's drunk."
Title:  
Title:     "It is, that! You try it!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck shuddered.
Title:  
Title:     "Well, no -- I reckon not."
Title:  
Title:     "And I reckon not, Huck. Only one bottle along-side of Injun Joe ain't
Title:  enough. If there'd been three, he'd be drunk enough and I'd do it."
Title:  
Title:     There was a long pause for reflection, and then Tom said:
Title:  
Title:     "Lookyhere, Huck, less not try that thing any more till we know Injun
Title:  Joe's not in there. It's too scary. Now, if we watch every night, we'll be
Title:  dead sure to see him go out, some time or other, and then we'll snatch that
Title:  box quicker'n lightning."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I'm agreed. I'll watch the whole night long, and I'll do it every
Title:  night, too, if you'll do the other part of the job."
Title:  
Title:     "All right, I will. All you got to do is to trot up Hooper Street a block
Title:  and maow -- and if I'm asleep, you throw some gravel at the window and
Title:  that'll fetch me."
Title:  
Title:     "Agreed, and good as wheat!"
Title:  
Title:     "Now, Huck, the storm's over, and I'll go
Title:  home. It'll begin to be daylight in a couple of hours. You go back and watch
Title:  that long, will you?"
Title:  
Title:     "I said I would, Tom, and I will. I'll ha'nt that tavern every night for
Title:  a year! I'll sleep all day and I'll stand watch all night."
Title:  
Title:     "That's all right. Now, where you going to sleep?"
Title:  
Title:     "In Ben Rogers' hayloft. He lets me, and so does his pap's nigger man,
Title:  Uncle Jake. I tote water for Uncle Jake whenever he wants me to, and any
Title:  time I ask him he gives me a little something to eat if he can spare it.
Title:  That's a mighty good nigger, Tom. He likes me, becuz I don't ever act as if
Title:  I was above him. Sometime I've set right down and eat with him. But you
Title:  needn't tell that. A body's got to do things when he's awful hungry he
Title:  wouldn't want to do as a steady thing."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, if I don't want you in the daytime, I'll let you sleep. I won't
Title:  come bothering around. Any time you see something's up, in the night, just
Title:  skip right around and maow."
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXIX
Title:  
Title:     THE first thing Tom heard on Friday morning was a glad piece of news --
Title:  Judge Thatcher's family had come back to town the night before. Both Injun
Title:  Joe and the treasure sunk into secondary importance for a moment, and Becky
Title:  took the chief place in the boy's interest. He saw her and they had an
Title:  exhausting good time playing "hispy" and "gully-keeper" with a crowd of
Title:  their school-mates. The day was completed and crowned in a peculiarly
Title:  satisfactory way: Becky teased her mother to appoint the next day for the
Title:  long-promised and long-delayed picnic, and she consented. The child's
Title:  delight was boundless; and Tom's not more moderate. The invitations were
Title:  sent out before sunset, and straightway the young folks of the village were
Title:  thrown into a fever of preparation and pleasurable anticipation. Tom's
Title:  excitement enabled him to keep awake until a pretty late hour, and he had
Title:  good hopes of hearing Huck's "maow," and of having his treasure to astonish
Title:  Becky and the picnickers with, next day; but he was disappointed. No signal
Title:  came that night.
Title:  
Title:     Morning came, eventually, and by ten or eleven o'clock a giddy and
Title:  rollicking company were gathered at Judge Thatcher's, and everything was
Title:  ready for a start. It was not the custom for elderly people to mar the
Title:  picnics with their presence. The children were considered safe enough under
Title:  the wings of a few young ladies of eighteen and a few young gentlemen of
Title:  twenty-three or thereabouts. The old steam ferry-boat was chartered for the
Title:  occasion; presently the gay throng filed up the main street laden with
Title:  provision-baskets. Sid was sick and had to miss the fun; Mary remained at
Title:  home to entertain him. The last thing Mrs. Thatcher said to Becky, was:
Title:  
Title:     "You'll not get back till late. Perhaps you'd better stay all night with
Title:  some of the girls that live near the ferry-landing, child."
Title:  
Title:     "Then I'll stay with Susy Harper, mamma."
Title:  
Title:     "Very well. And mind and behave yourself and don't be any trouble."
Title:  
Title:     Presently, as they tripped along, Tom said to Becky:
Title:  
Title:     "Say -- I'll tell you what we'll do. 'Stead of going to Joe Harper's
Title:  we'll climb right up the hill and stop at the Widow Douglas'. She'll have
Title:  ice-cream! She has it most every day -- dead loads of it. And she'll be
Title:  awful glad to have us."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, that will be fun!"
Title:  
Title:     Then Becky reflected a moment and said:
Title:  
Title:     "But what will mamma say?"
Title:  
Title:     "How'll she ever know?"
Title:  
Title:     The girl turned the idea over in her mind, and said reluctantly:
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon it's wrong -- but -- "
Title:  
Title:     "But shucks! Your mother won't know, and so what's the harm? All she
Title:  wants is that you'll be safe; and I bet you she'd 'a' said go there if she'd
Title:  'a' thought of it. I know she would!"
Title:  
Title:     The Widow Douglas' splendid hospitality was a tempting bait. It and Tom's
Title:  persuasions presently carried the day. So it was decided to say nothing
Title:  anybody about the night's programme. Presently it occurred to Tom that maybe
Title:  Huck might come this very night and give the signal. The thought took a deal
Title:  of the spirit out of his anticipations. Still he could not bear to give up
Title:  the fun at Widow Douglas'. And why should he give it up, he reasoned -- the
Title:  signal did not come the night before, so why should it be any more likely to
Title:  come to-night? The sure fun of the evening outweighed the uncertain
Title:  treasure; and, boy-like, he determined to yield to the stronger inclination
Title:  and not allow himself to think of the box of money another time that day.
Title:  
Title:     Three miles below town the ferryboat stopped at the mouth of a woody
Title:  hollow and tied up. The crowd swarmed ashore and soon the forest distances
Title:  and craggy heights echoed far and near with shoutings and laughter. All the
Title:  different ways of getting hot and tired were gone through with, and by and
Title:  by the rovers straggled back to camp fortified with responsible appetites,
Title:  and then the destruction of the good things began. After the feast there was
Title:  a refreshing season of rest and chat in the shade of spreading oaks.
Title:  By-and-by somebody shouted:
Title:  
Title:     "Who's ready for the cave?"
Title:  
Title:     Everybody was. Bundles of candles were procured, and straightway there
Title:  was a general scamper up the hill. The mouth of the cave was up the hillside
Title:  -- an opening shaped like a letter A. Its massive oaken door stood unbarred.
Title:  Within was a small chamber, chilly as an ice-house, and walled by Nature
Title:  with solid limestone that was dewy with a cold sweat. It was romantic and
Title:  mysterious to stand here in the deep gloom and look out upon the green
Title:  valley shining in the sun. But the impressiveness of the situation quickly
Title:  wore off, and the romping began again. The moment a candle was lighted there
Title:  was a general rush upon the owner of it; a struggle and a gallant defence
Title:  followed, but the candle was soon knocked down or blown out, and then there
Title:  was a glad clamor of laughter and a new chase. But all things have an end.
Title:  By-and-by the procession went filing down the steep descent of the main
Title:  avenue, the flickering rank of lights dimly revealing the lofty walls of
Title:  rock almost to their point of junction sixty feet overhead. This main avenue
Title:  was not more than eight or ten feet wide. Every few steps other lofty and
Title:  still narrower crevices branched from it on either hand -- for Mc-
Title:  Dougal's cave was but a vast labyrinth of crooked aisles that ran into each
Title:  other and out again and led nowhere. It was said that one might wander days
Title:  and nights together through its intricate tangle of rifts and chasms, and
Title:  never find the end of the cave; and that he might go down, and down, and
Title:  still down, into the earth, and it was just the same -- labyrinth under
Title:  labyrinth, and no end to any of them. No man "knew" the cave. That was an
Title:  impossible thing. Most of the young men knew a portion of it, and it was not
Title:  customary to venture much beyond this known portion. Tom Sawyer knew as much
Title:  of the cave as any one.
Title:  
Title:     The procession moved along the main avenue some three-quarters of a mile,
Title:  and then groups and couples began to slip aside into branch avenues, fly
Title:  along the dismal corridors, and take each other by surprise at points where
Title:  the corridors joined again. Parties were able to elude each other for the
Title:  space of half an hour without going beyond the "known" ground.
Title:  
Title:     By-and-by, one group after another came straggling back to the mouth of
Title:  the cave, panting, hilarious, smeared from head to foot with tallow
Title:  drippings, daubed with clay, and entirely delighted with the success of the
Title:  day. Then they were astonished to find that they had been taking no note of
Title:  time and that night was about at hand. The clanging bell had been calling
Title:  for half an hour. However, this sort of close to the day's adventures was
Title:  romantic and therefore satisfactory.When the ferryboat with her wild freight
Title:  pushed into the stream, nobody cared sixpence for the wasted time but the
Title:  captain of the craft.
Title:  
Title:     Huck was already upon his watch when the ferry-boat's lights went
Title:  glinting past the wharf. He heard no noise on board, for the young people
Title:  were as subdued and still as people usually are who are nearly tired to
Title:  death. He wondered what boat it was, and why she did not stop at the wharf
Title:  -- and then he dropped her out of his mind and put his attention upon his
Title:  business. The night was growing cloudy and dark. Ten o'clock came, and the
Title:  noise of vehicles ceased, scattered lights began to wink out, all straggling
Title:  foot-passengers disappeared, the village betook itself to its slumbers and
Title:  left the small watcher alone with the silence and the ghosts. Eleven o'clock
Title:  came, and the tavern lights were put out; darkness everywhere, now. Huck
Title:  waited what seemed a weary long time, but nothing happened. His faith was
Title:  weakening. Was there any use? Was there really any use? Why not give it up
Title:  and turn in?
Title:  
Title:     A noise fell upon his ear. He was all attention in an instant. The alley
Title:  door closed softly. He sprang to the corner of the brick store. The next
Title:  moment two men brushed by him, and one seemed to have something under his
Title:  arm. It must be that box! So they were going to remove the treasure. Why
Title:  call Tom now? It would be absurd -- the
Title:  men would get away with the box and never be found again. No, he would stick
Title:  to their wake and follow them; he would trust to the darkness for security
Title:  from discovery. So communing with himself, Huck stepped out and glided along
Title:  behind the men, cat-like, with bare feet, allowing them to keep just far
Title:  enough ahead not to be invisible.
Title:  
Title:     They moved up the river street three blocks, then turned to the left up a
Title:  cross-street. They went straight ahead, then, until they came to the path
Title:  that led up Cardiff Hill; this they took. They passed by the old Welshman's
Title:  house, half-way up the hill, without hesitating, and still climbed upward.
Title:  Good, thought Huck, they will bury it in the old quarry. But they never
Title:  stopped at the quarry. They passed on, up the summit. They plunged into the
Title:  narrow path between the tall sumach bushes, and were at once hidden in the
Title:  gloom. Huck closed up and shortened his distance, now, for they would never
Title:  be able to see him. He trotted along awhile; then slackened his pace,
Title:  fearing he was gaining too fast; moved on a piece, then stopped altogether;
Title:  listened; no sound; none, save that he seemed to hear the beating of his own
Title:  heart. The hooting of an owl came over the hill -- ominous sound! But no
Title:  footsteps. Heavens, was everything lost! He was about to spring with winged
Title:  feet, when a man cleared his throat not four feet from him! Huck's heart
Title:  shot into his throat, but he swallowed it again; and then he stood there
Title:  shaking as if a dozen agues had taken charge of him at once, and so weak
Title:  that he thought he must surely fall to the ground. He knew where he was. He
Title:  knew he was within five steps of the stile leading into Widow Douglas'
Title:  grounds. Very well, he thought, let them bury it there; it won't be hard to
Title:  find.
Title:  
Title:     Now there was a voice -- a very low voice -- Injun Joe's:
Title:  
Title:     "Damn her, maybe she's got company -- there's lights, late as it is."
Title:  
Title:     "I can't see any."
Title:  
Title:     This was that stranger's voice -- the stranger of the haunted house. A
Title:  deadly chill went to Huck's heart -- this, then, was the "revenge" job! His
Title:  thought was, to fly. Then he remembered that the Widow Douglas had been kind
Title:  to him more than once, and maybe these men were going to murder her. He
Title:  wished he dared venture to warn her; but he knew he didn't dare -- they
Title:  might come and catch him. He thought all this and more in the moment that
Title:  elapsed between the stranger's remark and Injun Joe's next -- which was --
Title:  
Title:     "Because the bush is in your way. Now -- this way -- now you see, don't
Title:  you?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes. Well, there is company there, I reckon. Better give it up."
Title:  
Title:     "Give it up, and I just leaving this country forever! Give it up and
Title:  maybe never have another chance. I tell you again, as I've told you before,
Title:  I don't care for her swag -- you may have it. But her husband was rough on me
Title:  -- many times he was rough on me -- and mainly he was the justice of the
Title:  peace that jugged me for a vagrant. And that ain't all. It ain't a millionth
Title:  part of it! He had me horsewhipped! -- horsewhipped in front of the jail,
Title:  like a nigger! -- with all the town looking on! HORSEWHIPPED! -- do you
Title:  understand? He took advantage of me and died. But I'll take it out of her."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, don't kill her! Don't do that!"
Title:  
Title:     "Kill? Who said anything about killing? I would kill him if he was here;
Title:  but not her. When you want to get revenge on a woman you don't kill her --
Title:  bosh! you go for her looks. You slit her nostrils -- you notch her ears like
Title:  a sow!"
Title:  
Title:     "By God, that's -- "
Title:  
Title:     "Keep your opinion to yourself! It will be safest for you. I'll tie her
Title:  to the bed. If she bleeds to death, is that my fault? I'll not cry, if she
Title:  does. My friend, you'll help me in this thing -- for MY sake -- that's why
Title:  you're here -- I mightn't be able alone. If you flinch, I'll kill you. Do
Title:  you understand that? And if I have to kill you, I'll kill her -- and then I
Title:  reckon nobody'll ever know much about who done this business."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, if it's got to be done, let's get at it. The quicker the better --
Title:  I'm all in a shiver."
Title:  
Title:     "Do it now? And company there? Look here -- I'll get suspicious of you,
Title:  first thing you know.  No -- we'll wait till the lights are out -- there's
Title:  no hurry."
Title:  
Title:     Huck felt that a silence was going to ensue -- a thing still more awful
Title:  than any amount of murderous talk; so he held his breath and stepped
Title:  gingerly back; planted his foot carefully and firmly, after balancing,
Title:  one-legged, in a precarious way and almost toppling over, first on one side
Title:  and then on the other. He took another step back, with the same elaboration
Title:  and the same risks; then another and another, and -- a twig snapped under
Title:  his foot! His breath stopped and he listened. There was no sound -- the
Title:  stillness was perfect. His gratitude was measureless. Now he turned in his
Title:  tracks, between the walls of sumach bushes -- turned himself as carefully as
Title:  if he were a ship -- and then stepped quickly but cautiously along. When he
Title:  emerged at the quarry he felt secure, and so he picked up his nimble heels
Title:  and flew. Down, down he sped, till he reached the Welshman's. He banged at
Title:  the door, and presently the heads of the old man and his two stalwart sons
Title:  were thrust from windows.
Title:  
Title:     "What's the row there? Who's banging? What do you want?"
Title:  
Title:     "Let me in -- quick! I'll tell everything."
Title:  
Title:     "Why, who are you?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huckleberry Finn -- quick, let me in!"
Title:  
Title:     "Huckleberry Finn, indeed! It ain't a name to open many doors, I judge!
Title:  But let him in, lads, and let's see what's the trouble."
Title:  
Title:     "Please don't ever tell I told you," were Huck's first words when he got
Title:  in. "Please don't -- I'd be killed, sure -- but the widow's been good
Title:  friends to me sometimes, and I want to tell -- I will tell if you'll promise
Title:  you won't ever say it was me."
Title:  
Title:     "By George, he has got something to tell, or he wouldn't act so!"
Title:  exclaimed the old man; "out with it and nobody here'll ever tell, lad."
Title:  
Title:     Three minutes later the old man and his sons, well armed, were up the
Title:  hill, and just entering the sumach path on tiptoe, their weapons in their
Title:  hands. Huck accompanied them no further. He hid behind a great bowlder and
Title:  fell to listening. There was a lagging, anxious silence, and then all of a
Title:  sudden there was an explosion of firearms and a cry.
Title:  
Title:     Huck waited for no particulars. He sprang away and sped down the hill as
Title:  fast as his legs could carry him.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                   Chapter XXX
Title:  
Title:     AS the earliest suspicion of dawn appeared on Sunday morning, Huck came
Title:  groping up the hill and rapped gently at the old Welshman's door. The
Title:  inmates were asleep, but it was a sleep that was set on a hair-trigger, on
Title:  account of the exciting episode of the night. A call came from a window:
Title:  
Title:     "Who's there!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck's scared voice answered in a low tone:
Title:  
Title:     "Please let me in! It's only Huck Finn!"
Title:  
Title:     "It's a name that can open this door night or day, lad! -- and welcome!"
Title:  
Title:     These were strange words to the vagabond boy's ears, and the pleasantest
Title:  he had ever heard. He could not recollect that the closing word had ever
Title:  been applied in his case before. The door was quickly unlocked, and he
Title:  entered. Huck was given a seat and the old man and his brace of tall sons
Title:  speedily dressed themselves.
Title:  
Title:     "Now, my boy, I hope you're good and hungry, because breakfast will be
Title:  ready as soon as the sun's up, and we'll have a piping hot one, too -- make
Title:  yourself easy about that! I and the boys hoped you'd turn up and stop here
Title:  last night."
Title:  
Title:     "I was awful scared," said Huck, "and I run. I took out when the pistols
Title:  went off, and I didn't stop for three mile. I've come now becuz I wanted to
Title:  know about it, you know; and I come before daylight becuz I didn't want to
Title:  run across them devils, even if they was dead."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, poor chap, you do look as if you'd had a hard night of it -- but
Title:  there's a bed here for you when you've had your breakfast. No, they ain't
Title:  dead, lad -- we are sorry enough for that. You see we knew right where to
Title:  put our hands on them, by your description; so we crept along on tiptoe till
Title:  we got within fifteen feet of them -- dark as a cellar that sumach path was
Title:  -- and just then I found I was going to sneeze. It was the meanest kind of
Title:  luck! I tried to keep it back, but no use -- 'twas bound to come, and it did
Title:  come! I was in the lead with my pistol raised, and when the sneeze started
Title:  those scoundrels a-rustling to get out of the path, I sung out, 'Fire boys!'
Title:  and blazed away at the place where the rustling was. So did the boys. But
Title:  they were off in a jiffy, those villains, and we after them, down through
Title:  the woods. I judge we never touched them. They fired a shot apiece as they
Title:  started, but their bullets whizzed by and didn't do us any harm. As soon as
Title:  we lost the sound of their feet we quit chasing, and went down and stirred
Title:  up the constables. They got a posse together, and went off to guard the
Title:  river bank, and as soon as it is light the sheriff and a gang are going to
Title:  beat up the
Title:  woods. My boys will be with them presently. I wish we had some sort of
Title:  description of those rascals -- 'twould help a good deal. But you couldn't
Title:  see what they were like, in the dark, lad, I suppose?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh yes; I saw them down-town and follered them."
Title:  
Title:     "Splendid! Describe them -- describe them, my boy!"
Title:  
Title:     "One's the old deaf and dumb Spaniard that's ben around here once or
Title:  twice, and t'other's a mean-looking, ragged -- "
Title:  
Title:     "That's enough, lad, we know the men! Happened on them in the woods back
Title:  of the widow's one day, and they slunk away. Off with you, boys, and tell
Title:  the sheriff -- get your breakfast to-morrow morning!"
Title:  
Title:     The Welshman's sons departed at once. As they were leaving the room Huck
Title:  sprang up and exclaimed:
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, please don't tell anybody it was me that blowed on them! Oh,
Title:  please!"
Title:  
Title:     "All right if you say it, Huck, but you ought to have the credit of what
Title:  you did."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh no, no! Please don't tell!"
Title:  
Title:     When the young men were gone, the old Welshman said:
Title:  
Title:     "They won't tell -- and I won't. But why don't you want it known?"
Title:  
Title:     Huck would not explain, further than to say that
Title:  he already knew too much about one of those men and would not have the man
Title:  know that he knew anything against him for the whole world -- he would be
Title:  killed for knowing it, sure.
Title:  
Title:     The old man promised secrecy once more, and said:
Title:  
Title:     "How did you come to follow these fellows, lad? Were they looking
Title:  suspicious?"
Title:  
Title:     Huck was silent while he framed a duly cautious reply. Then he said:
Title:  
Title:     "Well, you see, I'm a kind of a hard lot, -- least everybody says so, and
Title:  I don't see nothing agin it -- and sometimes I can't sleep much, on account
Title:  of thinking about it and sort of trying to strike out a new way of doing.
Title:  That was the way of it last night. I couldn't sleep, and so I come along
Title:  up-street 'bout midnight, a-turning it all over, and when I got to that old
Title:  shackly brick store by the Temperance Tavern, I backed up agin the wall to
Title:  have another think. Well, just then along comes these two chaps slipping
Title:  along close by me, with something under their arm, and I reckoned they'd
Title:  stole it. One was a-smoking, and t'other one wanted a light; so they stopped
Title:  right before me and the cigars lit up their faces and I see that the big one
Title:  was the deaf and dumb Spaniard, by his white whiskers and the patch on his
Title:  eye, and t'other one was a rusty, ragged-looking devil."
Title:  
Title:     "Could you see the rags by the light of the cigars?"
Title:  
Title:     This staggered Huck for a moment. Then he said:
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I don't know -- but somehow it seems as if I did."
Title:  
Title:     "Then they went on, and you -- "
Title:  
Title:     "Follered 'em -- yes. That was it. I wanted to see what was up -- they
Title:  sneaked along so. I dogged 'em to the widder's stile, and stood in the dark
Title:  and heard the ragged one beg for the widder, and the Spaniard swear he'd
Title:  spile her looks just as I told you and your two -- "
Title:  
Title:     "What! The deaf and dumb man said all that!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck had made another terrible mistake! He was trying his best to keep
Title:  the old man from getting the faintest hint of who the Spaniard might be, and
Title:  yet his tongue seemed determined to get him into trouble in spite of all he
Title:  could do. He made several efforts to creep out of his scrape, but the old
Title:  man's eye was upon him and he made blunder after blunder. Presently the
Title:  Welshman said:
Title:  
Title:     "My boy, don't be afraid of me. I wouldn't hurt a hair of your head for
Title:  all the world. No -- I'd protect you -- I'd protect you. This Spaniard is
Title:  not deaf and dumb; you've let that slip without intending it; you can't
Title:  cover that up now. You know something about that Spaniard that you want to
Title:  keep dark. Now trust me -- tell me what it is, and trust me -- I won't
Title:  betray you."
Title:  
Title:     Huck looked into the old man's honest eyes a moment, then bent over and
Title:  whispered in his ear:
Title:  
Title:     "'Tain't a Spaniard -- it's Injun Joe!"
Title:  
Title:     The Welshman almost jumped out of his chair. In a moment he said:
Title:  
Title:     "It's all plain enough, now. When you talked about notching ears and
Title:  slitting noses I judged that that was your own embellishment, because white
Title:  men don't take that sort of revenge. But an Injun! That's a different matter
Title:  altogether."
Title:  
Title:     During breakfast the talk went on, and in the course of it the old man
Title:  said that the last thing which he and his sons had done, before going to
Title:  bed, was to get a lantern and examine the stile and its vicinity for marks
Title:  of blood. They found none, but captured a bulky bundle of --
Title:  
Title:     "Of WHAT?"
Title:  
Title:     If the words had been lightning they could not have leaped with a more
Title:  stunning suddenness from Huck's blanched lips. His eyes were staring wide,
Title:  now, and his breath suspended -- waiting for the answer. The Welshman
Title:  started -- stared in return -- three seconds -- five seconds -- ten -- then
Title:  replied:
Title:  
Title:     "Of burglar's tools. Why, what's the matter with you?"
Title:  
Title:     Huck sank back, panting gently, but deeply, unutterably grateful. The
Title:  Welshman eyed him gravely, curiously -- and presently said:
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, burglar's tools. That appears to relieve you a good deal. But what
Title:  did give you that turn? What were you expecting we'd found?"
Title:  
Title:     Huck was in a close place -- the inquiring eye was upon him -- he would
Title:  have given anything for material for a plausible answer -- nothing suggested
Title:  itself -- the inquiring eye was boring deeper and deeper -- a senseless
Title:  reply offered -- there was no time to weigh it, so at a venture he uttered
Title:  it -- feebly:
Title:  
Title:     "Sunday-school books, maybe."
Title:  
Title:     Poor Huck was too distressed to smile, but the old man laughed loud and
Title:  joyously, shook up the details of his anatomy from head to foot, and ended
Title:  by saying that such a laugh was money in a-man's pocket, because it cut down
Title:  the doctor's bill like everything. Then he added:
Title:  
Title:     "Poor old chap, you're white and jaded -- you ain't well a bit -- no
Title:  wonder you're a little flighty and off your balance. But you'll come out of
Title:  it. Rest and sleep will fetch you out all right, I hope."
Title:  
Title:     Huck was irritated to think he had been such a goose and betrayed such a
Title:  suspicious excitement, for he had dropped the idea that the parcel brought
Title:  from the tavern was the treasure, as soon as he had heard the talk at the
Title:  widow's stile. He had only thought it was not the treasure, however -- he
Title:  had not known that it wasn't -- and so the suggestion of a captured bundle
Title:  was too much for his self-possession. But on the whole he felt glad the
Title:  little episode had happened, for now he knew beyond all question that that
Title:  bundle was not the bundle, and so his mind was at rest and exceedingly
Title:  comfortable.
Title:  In fact, everything seemed to be drifting just in the right direction, now;
Title:  the treasure must be still in No. 2, the men would be captured and jailed
Title:  that day, and he and Tom could seize the gold that night without any trouble
Title:  or any fear of interruption.
Title:  
Title:     Just as breakfast was completed there was a knock at the door. Huck
Title:  jumped for a hiding-place, for he had no mind to be connected even remotely
Title:  with the late event. The Welshman admitted several ladies and gentlemen,
Title:  among them the Widow Douglas, and noticed that groups of citizens were
Title:  climbing up the hill -- to stare at the stile. So the news had spread. The
Title:  Welshman had to tell the story of the night to the visitors. The widow's
Title:  gratitude for her preservation was outspoken.
Title:  
Title:     "Don't say a word about it, madam. There's another that you're more
Title:  beholden to than you are to me and my boys, maybe, but he don't allow me to
Title:  tell his name. We wouldn't have been there but for him."
Title:  
Title:     Of course this excited a curiosity so vast that it almost belittled the
Title:  main matter -- but the Welshman allowed it to eat into the vitals of his
Title:  visitors, and through them be transmitted to the whole town, for he refused
Title:  to part with his secret. When all else had been learned, the widow said:
Title:  
Title:     "I went to sleep reading in bed and slept straight through all that
Title:  noise. Why didn't you come and wake me?"
Title:  
Title:     "We judged it warn't worth while. Those fellows warn't likely to come
Title:  again -- they hadn't any tools left to work with, and what was the use of
Title:  waking you up and scaring you to death? My three negro men stood guard at
Title:  your house all the rest of the night. They've just come back."
Title:  
Title:     More visitors came, and the story had to be told and retold for a couple
Title:  of hours more.
Title:  
Title:     There was no Sabbath-school during day-school vacation, but everybody was
Title:  early at church. The stirring event was well canvassed. News came that not a
Title:  sign of the two villains had been yet discovered. When the sermon was
Title:  finished, Judge Thatcher's wife dropped alongside of Mrs. Harper as she
Title:  moved down the aisle with the crowd and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Is my Becky going to sleep all day? I just expected she would be tired
Title:  to death."
Title:  
Title:     "Your Becky?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes," with a startled look -- "didn't she stay with you last night?"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, no."
Title:  
Title:     Mrs. Thatcher turned pale, and sank into a pew, just as Aunt Polly,
Title:  talking briskly with a friend, passed by. Aunt Polly said:
Title:  
Title:     "Good-morning, Mrs. Thatcher. Good-morning, Mrs. Harper. I've got a boy
Title:  that's turned up missing. I reckon my Tom stayed at your house last night --
Title:  one of you. And now he's afraid to come to church. I've got to settle with
Title:  him."
Title:  
Title:     Mrs. Thatcher shook her head feebly and turned paler than ever.
Title:  
Title:     "He didn't stay with us," said Mrs. Harper, beginning to look uneasy. A
Title:  marked anxiety came into Aunt Polly's face.
Title:  
Title:     "Joe Harper, have you seen my Tom this morning?"
Title:  
Title:     "No'm."
Title:  
Title:     "When did you see him last?"
Title:  
Title:     Joe tried to remember, but was not sure he could say. The people had
Title:  stopped moving out of church. Whispers passed along, and a boding uneasiness
Title:  took possession of every countenance. Children were anxiously questioned,
Title:  and young teachers. They all said they had not noticed whether Tom and Becky
Title:  were on board the ferryboat on the homeward trip; it was dark; no one
Title:  thought of inquiring if any one was missing. One young man finally blurted
Title:  out his fear that they were still in the cave! Mrs. Thatcher swooned away.
Title:  Aunt Polly fell to crying and wringing her hands.
Title:  
Title:     The alarm swept from lip to lip, from group to group, from street to
Title:  street, and within five minutes the bells were wildly clanging and the whole
Title:  town was up! The Cardiff Hill episode sank into instant insignificance, the
Title:  burglars were forgotten, horses were saddled, skiffs were manned, the
Title:  ferryboat ordered out, and before the horror was half an hour old, two
Title:  hundred men were pouring down highroad and river toward the cave.
Title:  
Title:     All the long afternoon the village seemed empty and dead. Many women
Title:  visited Aunt Polly and Mrs. Thatcher and tried to comfort them. They cried
Title:  with them, too, and that was still better than words. All the tedious night
Title:  the town waited for news; but when the morning dawned at last, all the word
Title:  that came was, "Send more candles -- and send food." Mrs. Thatcher was
Title:  almost crazed; and Aunt Polly, also. Judge Thatcher sent messages of hope
Title:  and encouragement from the cave, but they conveyed no real cheer.
Title:  
Title:     The old Welshman came home toward daylight, spattered with candle-grease,
Title:  smeared with clay, and almost worn out. He found Huck still in the bed that
Title:  had been provided for him, and delirious with fever. The physicians were all
Title:  at the cave, so the Widow Douglas came and took charge of the patient. She
Title:  said she would do her best by him, because, whether he was good, bad, or
Title:  indifferent, he was the Lord's, and nothing that was the Lord's was a thing
Title:  to be neglected. The Welshman said Huck had good spots in him, and the widow
Title:  said:
Title:  
Title:     "You can depend on it. That's the Lord's mark. He don't leave it off. He
Title:  never does. Puts it somewhere on every creature that comes from his hands."
Title:  
Title:     Early in the forenoon parties of jaded men began to straggle into the
Title:  village, but the strongest of the citizens continued searching. All the news
Title:  that could be gained was that remotenesses of the cavern
Title:  were being ransacked that had never been visited before; that every corner
Title:  and crevice was going to be thoroughly searched; that wherever one wandered
Title:  through the maze of passages, lights were to be seen flitting hither and
Title:  thither in the distance, and shoutings and pistol-shots sent their hollow
Title:  reverberations to the ear down the sombre aisles. In one place, far from the
Title:  section usually traversed by tourists, the names "BECKY & TOM" had been
Title:  found traced upon the rocky wall with candle-smoke, and near at hand a
Title:  grease-soiled bit of ribbon. Mrs. Thatcher recognized the ribbon and cried
Title:  over it. She said it was the last relic she should ever have of her child;
Title:  and that no other memorial of her could ever be so precious, because this
Title:  one parted latest from the living body before the awful death came. Some
Title:  said that now and then, in the cave, a far-away speck of light would
Title:  glimmer, and then a glorious shout would burst forth and a score of men go
Title:  trooping down the echoing aisle -- and then a sickening disappointment
Title:  always followed; the children were not there; it was only a searcher's
Title:  light.
Title:  
Title:     Three dreadful days and nights dragged their tedious hours along, and the
Title:  village sank into a hopeless stupor. No one had heart for anything. The
Title:  accidental discovery, just made, that the proprietor of the Temperance
Title:  Tavern kept liquor on his premises, scarcely fluttered the public pulse,
Title:  tremendous as the fact was. In a lucid interval, Huck feebly led up to the
Title:  subject of taverns, and finally
Title:  asked -- dimly dreading the worst -- if anything had been discovered at the
Title:  Temperance Tavern since he had been ill.
Title:  
Title:     "Yes," said the widow.
Title:  
Title:     Huck started up in bed, wild-eyed:
Title:  
Title:     "What? What was it?"
Title:  
Title:     "Liquor! -- and the place has been shut up. Lie down, child -- what a
Title:  turn you did give me!"
Title:  
Title:     "Only tell me just one thing -- only just one -- please! Was it Tom
Title:  Sawyer that found it?"
Title:  
Title:     The widow burst into tears. "Hush, hush, child, hush! I've told you
Title:  before, you must not talk. You are very, very sick!"
Title:  
Title:     Then nothing but liquor had been found; there would have been a great
Title:  powwow if it had been the gold. So the treasure was gone forever -- gone
Title:  forever! But what could she be crying about? Curious that she should cry.
Title:  
Title:     These thoughts worked their dim way through Huck's mind, and under the
Title:  weariness they gave him he fell asleep. The widow said to herself:
Title:  
Title:     "There -- he's asleep, poor wreck. Tom Sawyer find it! Pity but somebody
Title:  could find Tom Sawyer! Ah, there ain't many left, now, that's got hope
Title:  enough, or strength enough, either, to go on searching."
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXXI
Title:  
Title:     NOW to return to Tom and Becky's share in the picnic. They tripped along
Title:  the murky aisles with the rest of the company, visiting the familiar wonders
Title:  of the cave -- wonders dubbed with rather over-descriptive names, such as
Title:  "The Drawing-Room," "The Cathedral," "Aladdin's Palace," and so on.
Title:  Presently the hide-and-seek frolicking began, and Tom and Becky engaged in
Title:  it with zeal until the exertion began to grow a trifle wearisome; then they
Title:  wandered down a sinuous avenue holding their candles aloft and reading the
Title:  tangled web-work of names, dates, post-office addresses, and mottoes with
Title:  which the rocky walls had been frescoed (in candle-smoke). Still drifting
Title:  along and talking, they scarcely noticed that they were now in a part of the
Title:  cave whose walls were not frescoed. They smoked their own names under an
Title:  overhanging shelf and moved on. Presently they came to a place where a
Title:  little stream of water, trickling over a ledge and carrying a limestone
