Title:  sediment with it, had, in the slow-dragging ages, formed a laced and ruffled
Title:  Niagara in gleaming and imperishable stone. Tom squeezed his small body
Title:  behind it in order to illuminate it for Becky's gratification.
Title:  He found that it curtained a sort of
Title:  steep natural stairway which was enclosed between narrow walls, and at once
Title:  the ambition to be a discoverer seized him. Becky responded to his call, and
Title:  they made a smoke-mark for future guidance, and started upon their quest.
Title:  They wound this way and that, far down into the secret depths of the cave,
Title:  made another mark, and branched off in search of novelties to tell the upper
Title:  world about. In one place they found a spacious cavern, from whose ceiling
Title:  depended a multitude of shining stalactites of the length and circumference
Title:  of a man's leg; they walked all about it, wondering and admiring, and
Title:  presently left it by one of the numerous passages that opened into it. This
Title:  shortly brought them to a bewitching spring, whose basin was incrusted with
Title:  a frostwork of glittering crystals; it was in the midst of a cavern whose
Title:  walls were supported by many fantastic pillars which had been formed by the
Title:  joining of great stalactites and stalagmites together, the result of the
Title:  ceaseless water-drip of centuries. Under the roof vast knots of bats had
Title:  packed themselves together, thousands in a bunch; the lights disturbed the
Title:  creatures and they came flocking down by hundreds, squeaking and darting
Title:  furiously at the candles. Tom knew their ways and the danger of this sort of
Title:  conduct. He seized Becky's hand and hurried her into the first corridor that
Title:  offered; and none too soon, for a bat struck Becky's light out with its wing
Title:  while she was passing
Title:  out of the cavern. The bats chased the children a good distance; but the
Title:  fugitives plunged into every new passage that offered, and at last got rid
Title:  of the perilous things. Tom found a subterranean lake, shortly, which
Title:  stretched its dim length away until its shape was lost in the shadows. He
Title:  wanted to explore its borders, but concluded that it would be best to sit
Title:  down and rest awhile, first. Now, for the first time, the deep stillness of
Title:  the place laid a clammy hand upon the spirits of the children. Becky said:
Title:  
Title:     "Why, I didn't notice, but it seems ever so long since I heard any of the
Title:  others."
Title:  
Title:     "Come to think, Becky, we are away down below them -- and I don't know
Title:  how far away north, or south, or east, or whichever it is. We couldn't hear
Title:  them here."
Title:  
Title:     Becky grew apprehensive.
Title:  
Title:     "I wonder how long we've been down here, Tom? We better start back."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, I reckon we better. P'raps we better."
Title:  
Title:     "Can you find the way, Tom? It's all a mixed-up crookedness to me."
Title:  
Title:     "I reckon I could find it -- but then the bats. If they put our candles
Title:  out it will be an awful fix. Let's try some other way, so as not to go
Title:  through there."
Title:  
Title:     "Well. But I hope we won't get lost. It would be so awful!" and the girl
Title:  shuddered at the thought of the dreadful possibilities.
Title:  
Title:     They started through a corridor, and traversed it in silence a long way,
Title:  glancing at each new opening, to see if there was anything familiar about
Title:  the look of it; but they were all strange. Every time Tom made an
Title:  examination, Becky would watch his face for an encouraging sign, and he
Title:  would say cheerily:
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, it's all right. This ain't the one, but we'll come to it right
Title:  away!"
Title:  
Title:     But he felt less and less hopeful with each failure, and presently began
Title:  to turn off into diverging avenues at sheer random, in desperate hope of
Title:  finding the one that was wanted. He still said it was "all right," but there
Title:  was such a leaden dread at his heart that the words had lost their ring and
Title:  sounded just as if he had said, "All is lost!" Becky clung to his side in an
Title:  anguish of fear, and tried hard to keep back the tears, but they would come.
Title:  At last she said:
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, Tom, never mind the bats, let's go back that way! We seem to get
Title:  worse and worse off all the time."
Title:  
Title:     "Listen!" said he.
Title:  
Title:     Profound silence; silence so deep that even their breathings were
Title:  conspicuous in the hush. Tom shouted. The call went echoing down the empty
Title:  aisles and died out in the distance in a faint sound that resembled a ripple
Title:  of mocking laughter.
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, don't do it again, Tom, it is too horrid," said Becky.
Title:  
Title:     "It is horrid, but I better, Becky; they might hear us, you know," and he
Title:  shouted again.
Title:  
Title:     The "might" was even a chillier horror than the ghostly laughter, it so
Title:  confessed a perishing hope. The children stood still and listened; but there
Title:  was no result. Tom turned upon the back track at once, and hurried his
Title:  steps. It was but a little while before a certain indecision in his manner
Title:  revealed another fearful fact to Becky -- he could not find his way back!
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, Tom, you didn't make any marks!"
Title:  
Title:     "Becky, I was such a fool! Such a fool! I never thought we might want to
Title:  come back! No -- I can't find the way. It's all mixed up."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, Tom, we're lost! we're lost! We never can get out of this awful
Title:  place! Oh, why did we ever leave the others!"
Title:  
Title:     She sank to the ground and burst into such a frenzy of crying that Tom
Title:  was appalled with the idea that she might die, or lose her reason. He sat
Title:  down by her and put his arms around her; she buried her face in his bosom,
Title:  she clung to him, she poured out her terrors, her unavailing regrets, and
Title:  the far echoes turned them all to jeering laughter. Tom begged her to pluck
Title:  up hope again, and she said she could not. He fell to blaming and abusing
Title:  himself for getting her into this miserable situation; this had a better
Title:  effect. She said she would try to hope again, she would get up and follow
Title:  wherever he might lead if only he would not talk like that any
Title:  more. For he was no more to blame than she, she said.
Title:  
Title:     So they moved on again -- aimlessly -- simply at random -- all they could
Title:  do was to move, keep moving. For a little while, hope made a show of
Title:  reviving -- not with any reason to back it, but only because it is its
Title:  nature to revive when the spring has not been taken out of it by age and
Title:  familiarity with failure.
Title:  
Title:     By-and-by Tom took Becky's candle and blew it out. This economy meant so
Title:  much! Words were not needed. Becky understood, and her hope died again. She
Title:  knew that Tom had a whole candle and three or four pieces in his pockets --
Title:  yet he must economize.
Title:  
Title:     By-and-by, fatigue began to assert its claims; the children tried to pay
Title:  attention, for it was dreadful to think of sitting down when time was grown
Title:  to be so precious, moving, in some direction, in any direction, was at least
Title:  progress and might bear fruit; but to sit down was to invite death and
Title:  shorten its pursuit.
Title:  
Title:     At last Becky's frail limbs refused to carry her farther. She sat down.
Title:  Tom rested with her, and they talked of home, and the friends there, and the
Title:  comfortable beds and, above all, the light! Becky cried, and Tom tried to
Title:  think of some way of comforting her, but all his encouragements were grown
Title:  threadbare with use, and sounded like sarcasms. Fatigue bore so heavily upon
Title:  Becky that she drowsed
Title:  off to sleep. Tom was grateful. He sat looking into her drawn face and saw
Title:  it grow smooth and natural under the influence of pleasant dreams; and
Title:  by-and-by a smile dawned and rested there. The peaceful face reflected
Title:  somewhat of peace and healing into his own spirit, and his thoughts wandered
Title:  away to bygone times and dreamy memories. While he was deep in his musings,
Title:  Becky woke up with a breezy little laugh -- but it was stricken dead upon
Title:  her lips, and a groan followed it.
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, how could I sleep! I wish I never, never had waked! No! No, I don't,
Title:  Tom! Don't look so! I won't say it again."
Title:  
Title:     "I'm glad you've slept, Becky; you'll feel rested, now, and we'll find
Title:  the way out."
Title:  
Title:     "We can try, Tom; but I've seen such a beautiful country in my dream. I
Title:  reckon we are going there."
Title:  
Title:     "Maybe not, maybe not. Cheer up, Becky, and let's go on trying."
Title:  
Title:     They rose up and wandered along, hand in hand and hopeless. They tried to
Title:  estimate how long they had been in the cave, but all they knew was that it
Title:  seemed days and weeks, and yet it was plain that this could not be, for
Title:  their candles were not gone yet. A long time after this -- they could not
Title:  tell how long -- Tom said they must go softly and listen for dripping water
Title:  -- they must find a spring. They found one presently, and Tom said it was
Title:  time to rest again. Both were cruelly tired, yet Becky said
Title:  she thought she could go a little farther. She was surprised to hear Tom
Title:  dissent. She could not understand it. They sat down, and Tom fastened his
Title:  candle to the wall in front of them with some clay. Thought was soon busy;
Title:  nothing was said for some time. Then Becky broke the silence:
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, I am so hungry!"
Title:  
Title:     Tom took something out of his pocket.
Title:  
Title:     "Do you remember this?" said he.
Title:  
Title:     Becky almost smiled.
Title:  
Title:     "It's our wedding-cake, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes -- I wish it was as big as a barrel, for it's all we've got."
Title:  
Title:     "I saved it from the picnic for us to dream on, Tom, the way grown-up
Title:  people do with wedding-cake -- but it'll be our -- "
Title:  
Title:     She dropped the sentence where it was. Tom divided the cake and Becky ate
Title:  with good appetite, while Tom nibbled at his moiety. There was abundance of
Title:  cold water to finish the feast with. By-and-by Becky suggested that they
Title:  move on again. Tom was silent a moment. Then he said:
Title:  
Title:     "Becky, can you bear it if I tell you something?"
Title:  
Title:     Becky's face paled, but she thought she could.
Title:  
Title:     "Well, then, Becky, we must stay here, where there's water to drink. That
Title:  little piece is our last candle!"
Title:  
Title:     Becky gave loose to tears and wailings. Tom did what he could to comfort
Title:  her, but with little effect. At length Becky said:
Title:  
Title:     "Tom!"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, Becky?"
Title:  
Title:     "They'll miss us and hunt for us!"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, they will! Certainly they will!"
Title:  
Title:     "Maybe they're hunting for us now, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "Why, I reckon maybe they are. I hope they are."
Title:  
Title:     "When would they miss us, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "When they get back to the boat, I reckon."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, it might be dark then -- would they notice we hadn't come?"
Title:  
Title:     "I don't know. But anyway, your mother would miss you as soon as they got
Title:  home."
Title:  
Title:     A frightened look in Becky's face brought Tom to his senses and he saw
Title:  that he had made a blunder. Becky was not to have gone home that night! The
Title:  children became silent and thoughtful. In a moment a new burst of grief from
Title:  Becky showed Tom that the thing in his mind had struck hers also -- that the
Title:  Sabbath morning might be half spent before Mrs. Thatcher discovered that
Title:  Becky was not at Mrs. Harper's.
Title:  
Title:     The children fastened their eyes upon their bit of candle and watched it
Title:  melt slowly and pitilessly away; saw the half inch of wick stand alone at
Title:  last; saw the feeble flame rise and fall, climb the thin column of smoke,
Title:  linger at its top a moment, and then -- the horror of utter darkness
Title:  reigned!
Title:  
Title:     How long afterward it was that Becky came to a slow consciousness that
Title:  she was crying in Tom's
Title:  arms, neither could tell. All that they knew was, that after what seemed a
Title:  mighty stretch of time, both awoke out of a dead stupor of sleep and resumed
Title:  their miseries once more. Tom said it might be Sunday, now -- maybe Monday.
Title:  He tried to get Becky to talk, but her sorrows were too oppressive, all her
Title:  hopes were gone. Tom said that they must have been missed long ago, and no
Title:  doubt the search was going on. He would shout and maybe some one would come.
Title:  He tried it; but in the darkness the distant echoes sounded so hideously
Title:  that he tried it no more.
Title:  
Title:     The hours wasted away, and hunger came to torment the captives again. A
Title:  portion of Tom's half of the cake was left; they divided and ate it. But
Title:  they seemed hungrier than before. The poor morsel of food only whetted
Title:  desire.
Title:  
Title:     By-and-by Tom said:
Title:  
Title:     " Sh! Did you hear that?"
Title:  
Title:     Both held their breath and listened. There was a sound like the faintest,
Title:  far-off shout. Instantly Tom answered it, and leading Becky by the hand,
Title:  started groping down the corridor in its direction. Presently he listened
Title:  again; again the sound was heard, and apparently a little nearer.
Title:  
Title:     "It's them!" said Tom; "they're coming! Come along, Becky -- we're all
Title:  right now!"
Title:  
Title:     The joy of the prisoners was almost overwhelming. Their speed was slow,
Title:  however, because pitfalls were somewhat common, and had to be guarded
Title:  against. They shortly came to one and had to stop. It might be three feet
Title:  deep, it might be a hundred -- there was no passing it at any rate. Tom got
Title:  down on his breast and reached as far down as he could. No bottom. They must
Title:  stay there and wait until the searchers came. They listened; evidently the
Title:  distant shoutings were growing more distant! a moment or two more and they
Title:  had gone altogether. The heart-sinking misery of it! Tom whooped until he
Title:  was hoarse, but it was of no use. He talked hopefully to Becky; but an age
Title:  of anxious waiting passed and no sounds came again.
Title:  
Title:     The children groped their way back to the spring. The weary time dragged
Title:  on; they slept again, and awoke famished and woe-stricken. Tom believed it
Title:  must be Tuesday by this time.
Title:  
Title:     Now an idea struck him. There were some side passages near at hand. It
Title:  would be better to explore some of these than bear the weight of the heavy
Title:  time in idleness. He took a kite-line from his pocket, tied it to a
Title:  projection, and he and Becky started, Tom in the lead, unwinding the line as
Title:  he groped along. At the end of twenty steps the corridor ended in a
Title:  "jumping-off place." Tom got down on his knees and felt below, and then as
Title:  far around the corner as he could reach with his hands conveniently; he made
Title:  an effort to stretch yet a little farther to the right, and at that moment,
Title:  not twenty yards away, a human hand, holding a candle, appeared from behind
Title:  a rock! Tom lifted up a
Title:  glorious shout, and instantly that hand was followed by the body it belonged
Title:  to -- Injun Joe's! Tom was paralyzed; he could not move. He was vastly
Title:  gratified the next moment, to see the "Spaniard" take to his heels and get
Title:  himself out of sight. Tom wondered that Joe had not recognized his voice and
Title:  come over and killed him for testifying in court. But the echoes must have
Title:  disguised the voice. Without doubt, that was it, he reasoned. Tom's fright
Title:  weakened every muscle in his body. He said to himself that if he had
Title:  strength enough to get back to the spring he would stay there, and nothing
Title:  should tempt him to run the risk of meeting Injun Joe again. He was careful
Title:  to keep from Becky what it was he had seen. He told her he had only shouted
Title:  "for luck."
Title:  
Title:     But hunger and wretchedness rise superior to fears in the long run.
Title:  Another tedious wait at the spring and another long sleep brought changes.
Title:  The children awoke tortured with a raging hunger. Tom believed that it must
Title:  be Wednesday or Thursday or even Friday or Saturday, now, and that the
Title:  search had been given over. He proposed to explore another passage. He felt
Title:  willing to risk Injun Joe and all other terrors. But Becky was very weak.
Title:  She had sunk into a dreary apathy and would not be roused. She said she
Title:  would wait, now, where she was, and die -- it would not be long. She told
Title:  Tom to go with the kite-line and explore if he chose; but she implored him
Title:  to come back every
Title:  little while and speak to her; and she made him promise that when the awful
Title:  time came, he would stay by her and hold her hand until all was over.
Title:  
Title:     Tom kissed her, with a choking sensation in his throat, and made a show
Title:  of being confident of finding the searchers or an escape from the cave; then
Title:  he took the kite-line in his hand and went groping down one of the passages
Title:  on his hands and knees, distressed with hunger and sick with bodings of
Title:  coming doom.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXXII
Title:  
Title:     TUESDAY afternoon came, and waned to the twilight. The village of St.
Title:  Petersburg still mourned. The lost children had not been found. Public
Title:  prayers had been offered up for them, and many and many a private prayer
Title:  that had the petitioner's whole heart in it; but still no good news came
Title:  from the cave. The majority of the searchers had given up the quest and gone
Title:  back to their daily avocations, saying that it was plain the children could
Title:  never be found. Mrs. Thatcher was very ill, and a great part of the time
Title:  delirious. People said it was heartbreaking to hear her call her child, and
Title:  raise her head and listen a whole minute at a time, then lay it wearily down
Title:  again with a moan. Aunt Polly had drooped into a settled melancholy, and her
Title:  gray hair had grown almost white. The village went to its rest on Tuesday
Title:  night, sad and forlorn.
Title:  
Title:     Away in the middle of the night a wild peal burst from the village bells,
Title:  and in a moment the streets were swarming with frantic half-clad people, who
Title:  shouted, "Turn out! turn out! they're found! they're found!" Tin pans and
Title:  horns were added
Title:  to the din, the population massed itself and moved toward the river, met the
Title:  children coming in an open carriage drawn by shouting citizens, thronged
Title:  around it, joined its homeward march, and swept magnificently up the main
Title:  street roaring huzzah after huzzah!
Title:  
Title:     The village was illuminated; nobody went to bed again; it was the
Title:  greatest night the little town had ever seen. During the first half-hour a
Title:  procession of villagers filed through Judge Thatcher's house, seized the
Title:  saved ones and kissed them, squeezed Mrs. Thatcher's hand, tried to speak
Title:  but couldn't -- and drifted out raining tears all over the place.
Title:  
Title:     Aunt Polly's happiness was complete, and Mrs. Thatcher's nearly so. It
Title:  would be complete, however, as soon as the messenger dispatched with the
Title:  great news to the cave should get the word to her husband. Tom lay upon a
Title:  sofa with an eager auditory about him and told the history of the wonderful
Title:  adventure, putting in many striking additions to adorn it withal; and closed
Title:  with a description of how he left Becky and went on an exploring expedition;
Title:  how he followed two avenues as far as his kite-line would reach; how he
Title:  followed a third to the fullest stretch of the kite-line, and was about to
Title:  turn back when he glimpsed a far-off speck that looked like daylight;
Title:  dropped the line and groped toward it, pushed his head and shoulders through
Title:  a small hole, and saw the broad Mississippi rolling by! And if it had only
Title:  happened to be
Title:  night he would not have seen that speck of daylight and would not have
Title:  explored that passage any more! He told how he went back for Becky and broke
Title:  the good news and she told him not to fret her with such stuff, for she was
Title:  tired, and knew she was going to die, and wanted to. He described how he
Title:  labored with her and convinced her; and how she almost died for joy when she
Title:  had groped to where she actually saw the blue speck of daylight; how he
Title:  pushed his way out at the hole and then helped her out; how they sat there
Title:  and cried for gladness; how some men came along in a skiff and Tom hailed
Title:  them and told them their situation and their famished condition; how the men
Title:  didn't believe the wild tale at first, "because," said they, "you are five
Title:  miles down the river below the valley the cave is in" -- then took them
Title:  aboard, rowed to a house, gave them supper, made them rest till two or three
Title:  hours after dark and then brought them home.
Title:  
Title:     Before day-dawn, Judge Thatcher and the handful of searchers with him
Title:  were tracked out, in the cave, by the twine clews they had strung behind
Title:  them, and informed of the great news.
Title:  
Title:     Three days and nights of toil and hunger in the cave were not to be
Title:  shaken off at once, as Tom and Becky soon discovered. They were bedridden
Title:  all of Wednesday and Thursday, and seemed to grow more and more tired and
Title:  worn, all the time. Tom got about, a little, on Thursday, was down-town
Title:  Friday,
Title:  and nearly as whole as ever Saturday; but Becky did not leave her room until
Title:  Sunday, and then she looked as if she had passed through a wasting illness.
Title:  
Title:     Tom learned of Huck's sickness and went to see him on Friday, but could
Title:  not be admitted to the bedroom; neither could he on Saturday or Sunday. He
Title:  was admitted daily after that, but was warned to keep still about his
Title:  adventure and introduce no exciting topic. The Widow Douglas stayed by to
Title:  see that he obeyed. At home Tom learned of the Cardiff Hill event; also that
Title:  the "ragged man's" body had eventually been found in the river near the
Title:  ferry-landing; he had been drowned while trying to escape, perhaps.
Title:  
Title:     About a fortnight after Tom's rescue from the cave, he started off to
Title:  visit Huck, who had grown plenty strong enough, now, to hear exciting talk,
Title:  and Tom had some that would interest him, he thought. Judge Thatcher's house
Title:  was on Tom's way, and he stopped to see Becky. The Judge and some friends
Title:  set Tom to talking, and some one asked him ironically if he wouldn't like to
Title:  go to the cave again. Tom said he thought he wouldn't mind it. The Judge
Title:  said:
Title:  
Title:     "Well, there are others just like you, Tom, I've not the least doubt. But
Title:  we have taken care of that. Nobody will get lost in that cave any more."
Title:  
Title:     "Why?"
Title:  
Title:     "Because I had its big door sheathed with boiler
Title:  iron two weeks ago, and triple-locked -- and I've got the keys."
Title:  
Title:     Tom turned as white as a sheet.
Title:  
Title:     "What's the matter, boy! Here, run, somebody! Fetch a glass of water!"
Title:  
Title:     The water was brought and thrown into Tom's face.
Title:  
Title:     "Ah, now you're all right. What was the matter with you, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, Judge, Injun Joe's in the cave!"
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                 Chapter XXXIII
Title:  
Title:     WITHIN a few minutes the news had spread, and a dozen skiff-loads of men
Title:  were on their way to McDougal's cave, and the ferryboat, well filled with
Title:  passengers, soon followed. Tom Sawyer was in the skiff that bore Judge
Title:  Thatcher.
Title:  
Title:     When the cave door was unlocked, a sorrowful sight presented itself in
Title:  the dim twilight of the place. Injun Joe lay stretched upon the ground,
Title:  dead, with his face close to the crack of the door, as if his longing eyes
Title:  had been fixed, to the latest moment, upon the light and the cheer of the
Title:  free world outside. Tom was touched, for he knew by his own experience how
Title:  this wretch had suffered. His pity was moved, but nevertheless he felt an
Title:  abounding sense of relief and security, now, which revealed to him in a
Title:  degree which he had not fully appreciated before how vast a weight of dread
Title:  had been lying upon him since the day he lifted his voice against this
Title:  bloody-minded outcast.
Title:  
Title:     Injun Joe's bowie-knife lay close by, its blade broken in two. The great
Title:  foundation-beam of the door had been chipped and hacked through, with
Title:  tedious labor; useless labor, too, it was, for the native rock formed a sill
Title:  outside it, and upon that stubborn material the knife had wrought no effect;
Title:  the only damage done was to the knife itself. But if there had been no stony
Title:  obstruction there the labor would have been useless still, for if the beam
Title:  had been wholly cut away Injun Joe could not have squeezed his body under
Title:  the door, and he knew it. So he had only hacked that place in order to be
Title:  doing something -- in order to pass the weary time -- in order to employ his
Title:  tortured faculties. Ordinarily one could find half a dozen bits of candle
Title:  stuck around in the crevices of this vestibule, left there by tourists; but
Title:  there were none now. The prisoner had searched them out and eaten them. He
Title:  had also contrived to catch a few bats, and these, also, he had eaten,
Title:  leaving only their claws. The poor unfortunate had starved to death. In one
Title:  place, near at hand, a stalagmite had been slowly growing up from the ground
Title:  for ages, builded by the water-drip from a stalactite overhead. The captive
Title:  had broken off the stalagmite, and upon the stump had placed a stone,
Title:  wherein he had scooped a shallow hollow to catch the precious drop that fell
Title:  once in every three minutes with the dreary regularity of a clock-tick -- a
Title:  dessertspoonful once in four and twenty hours. That drop was falling when
Title:  the Pyramids were new; when Troy fell; when the foundations of Rome were
Title:  laid when Christ was crucified; when the Conqueror created
Title:  the British empire; when Columbus sailed; when the massacre at Lexington was
Title:  "news." It is falling now; it will still be falling when all these things
Title:  shall have sunk down the afternoon of history, and the twilight of
Title:  tradition, and been swallowed up in the thick night of oblivion. Has
Title:  everything a purpose and a mission? Did this drop fall patiently during five
Title:  thousand years to be ready for this flitting human insect's need? and has it
Title:  another important object to accomplish ten thousand years to come? No
Title:  matter. It is many and many a year since the hapless half-breed scooped out
Title:  the stone to catch the priceless drops, but to this day the tourist stares
Title:  longest at that pathetic stone and that slow-dropping water when he comes to
Title:  see the wonders of McDougal's cave. Injun Joe's cup stands first in the list
Title:  of the cavern's marvels; even "Aladdin's Palace" cannot rival it.
Title:  
Title:     Injun Joe was buried near the mouth of the cave; and people flocked there
Title:  in boats and wagons from the towns and from all the farms and hamlets for
Title:  seven miles around; they brought their children, and all sorts of
Title:  provisions, and confessed that they had had almost as satisfactory a time at
Title:  the funeral as they could have had at the hanging.
Title:  
Title:     This funeral stopped the further growth of one thing -- the petition to
Title:  the governor for Injun Joe's pardon. The petition had been largely signed;
Title:  many tearful and eloquent meetings had been held, and a committee of sappy
Title:  women been appointed to
Title:  go in deep mourning and wail around the governor, and implore him to be a
Title:  merciful ass and trample his duty under foot. Injun Joe was believed to have
Title:  killed five citizens of the village, but what of that? If he had been Satan
Title:  himself there would have been plenty of weaklings ready to scribble their
Title:  names to a pardon-petition, and drip a tear on it from their permanently
Title:  impaired and leaky water-works.
Title:  
Title:     The morning after the funeral Tom took Huck to a private place to have an
Title:  important talk. Huck had learned all about Tom's adventure from the Welshman
Title:  and the Widow Douglas, by this time, but Tom said he reckoned there was one
Title:  thing they had not told him; that thing was what he wanted to talk about
Title:  now. Huck's face saddened. He said:
Title:  
Title:     "I know what it is. You got into No. 2 and never found anything but
Title:  whiskey. Nobody told me it was you; but I just knowed it must 'a' ben you,
Title:  soon as I heard 'bout that whiskey business; and I knowed you hadn't got the
Title:  money becuz you'd 'a' got at me some way or other and told me even if you
Title:  was mum to everybody else. Tom, something's always told me we'd never get
Title:  holt of that swag."
Title:  
Title:     "Why, Huck, I never told on that tavern-keeper. You know his tavern was
Title:  all right the Saturday I went to the picnic. Don't you remember you was to
Title:  watch there that night?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh yes! Why, it seems 'bout a year ago. It
Title:  was that very night that I follered Injun Joe to the widder's."
Title:  
Title:     "You followed him?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes -- but you keep mum. I reckon Injun Joe's left friends behind him,
Title:  and I don't want 'em souring on me and doing me mean tricks. If it hadn't
Title:  ben for me he'd be down in Texas now, all right."
Title:  
Title:     Then Huck told his entire adventure in confidence to Tom, who had only
Title:  heard of the Welshman's part of it before.
Title:  
Title:     "Well," said Huck, presently, coming back to the main question, "whoever
Title:  nipped the whiskey in No. 2, nipped the money, too, I reckon -- anyways it's
Title:  a goner for us, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, that money wasn't ever in No. 2!"
Title:  
Title:     "What!" Huck searched his comrade's face keenly. "Tom, have you got on
Title:  the track of that money again?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, it's in the cave!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck's eyes blazed.
Title:  
Title:     "Say it again, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "The money's in the cave!"
Title:  
Title:     "Tom -- honest injun, now -- is it fun, or earnest?"
Title:  
Title:     "Earnest, Huck -- just as earnest as ever I was in my life. Will you go
Title:  in there with me and help get it out?"
Title:  
Title:     "I bet I will! I will if it's where we can blaze our way to it and not
Title:  get lost."
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, we can do that without the least little bit of trouble in the
Title:  world."
Title:  
Title:     "Good as wheat! What makes you think the money's -- "
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, you just wait till we get in there. If we don't find it I'll agree
Title:  to give you my drum and every thing I've got in the world. I will, by
Title:  jings."
Title:  
Title:     "All right -- it's a whiz. When do you say?"
Title:  
Title:     "Right now, if you say it. Are you strong enough?"
Title:  
Title:     "Is it far in the cave? I ben on my pins a little, three or four days,
Title:  now, but I can't walk more'n a mile, Tom -- least I don't think I could."
Title:  
Title:     "It's about five mile into there the way anybody but me would go, Huck,
Title:  but there's a mighty short cut that they don't anybody but me know about.
Title:  Huck, I'll take you right to it in a skiff. I'll float the skiff down there,
Title:  and I'll pull it back again all by myself. You needn't ever turn your hand
Title:  over."
Title:  
Title:     "Less start right off, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "All right. We want some bread and meat, and our pipes, and a little bag
Title:  or two, and two or three kite-strings, and some of these new-fangled things
Title:  they call lucifer matches. I tell you, many's the time I wished I had some
Title:  when I was in there before."
Title:  
Title:     A trifle after noon the boys borrowed a small skiff from a citizen who
Title:  was absent, and got under way at once. When they were several miles below
Title:  "Cave Hollow," Tom said:
Title:  
Title:     "Now you see this bluff here looks all alike all the way down from the
Title:  cave hollow -- no houses, no wood-yards, bushes all alike. But do you see
Title:  that white place up yonder where there's been a landslide? Well, that's one
Title:  of my marks. We'll get ashore, now."
Title:  
Title:     They landed.
Title:  
Title:     "Now, Huck, where we're a-standing you could touch that hole I got out of
Title:  with a fishing-pole. See if you can find it."
Title:  
Title:     Huck searched all the place about, and found nothing. Tom proudly marched
Title:  into a thick clump of sumach bushes and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Here you are! Look at it, Huck; it's the snuggest hole in this country.
Title:  You just keep mum about it. All along I've been wanting to be a robber, but
Title:  I knew I'd got to have a thing like this, and where to run across it was the
Title:  bother. We've got it now, and we'll keep it quiet, only we'll let Joe Harper
Title:  and Ben Rogers in -- because of course there's got to be a Gang, or else
Title:  there wouldn't be any style about it. Tom Sawyer's Gang -- it sounds
Title:  splendid, don't it, Huck?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, it just does, Tom. And who'll we rob?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, most anybody. Waylay people -- that's mostly the way."
Title:  
Title:     "And kill them?"
Title:  
Title:     "No, not always. Hive them in the cave till they raise a ransom."
Title:  
Title:     "What's a ransom?"
Title:  
Title:     "Money. You make them raise all they can, off'n their friends; and after
Title:  you've kept them a year, if it ain't raised then you kill them. That's the
Title:  general way. Only you don't kill the women. You shut up the women, but you
Title:  don't kill them. They're always beautiful and rich, and awfully scared. You
Title:  take their watches and things, but you always take your hat off and talk
Title:  polite. They ain't anybody as polite as robbers -- you'll see that in any
Title:  book. Well, the women get to loving you, and after they've been in the cave
Title:  a week or two weeks they stop crying and after that you couldn't get them to
Title:  leave. If you drove them out they'd turn right around and come back. It's so
Title:  in all the books."
Title:  
Title:     "Why, it's real bully, Tom. I believe it's better'n to be a pirate."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, it's better in some ways, because it's close to home and circuses
Title:  and all that."
Title:  
Title:     By this time everything was ready and the boys entered the hole, Tom in
Title:  the lead. They toiled their way to the farther end of the tunnel, then made
Title:  their spliced kite-strings fast and moved on. A few steps brought them to
Title:  the spring, and Tom felt a shudder quiver all through him. He showed Huck
Title:  the fragment of candle-wick perched on a lump of clay against the wall, and
Title:  described how he and Becky had watched the flame struggle and expire.
Title:  
Title:     The boys began to quiet down to whispers, now, for the stillness and
Title:  gloom of the place oppressed
Title:  their spirits. They went on, and presently entered and followed Tom's other
Title:  corridor until they reached the "jumping-off place." The candles revealed
Title:  the fact that it was not really a precipice, but only a steep clay hill
Title:  twenty or thirty feet high. Tom whispered:
Title:  
Title:     "Now I'll show you something, Huck."
Title:  
Title:     He held his candle aloft and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Look as far around the corner as you can. Do you see that? There -- on
Title:  the big rock over yonder -- done with candle-smoke."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, it's a cross!"
Title:  
Title:     "NOW where's your Number Two? 'Under the cross,' hey? Right yonder'swhere
Title:  I saw Injun Joe poke up his candle, Huck!"
Title:  
Title:     Huck stared at the mystic sign awhile, and then said with a shaky voice:
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, less git out of here!"
Title:  
Title:     "What! and leave the treasure?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes -- leave it. Injun Joe's ghost is round about there, certain."
Title:  
Title:     "No it ain't, Huck, no it ain't. It would ha'nt the place where he died
Title:  -- away out at the mouth of the cave -- five mile from here."
Title:  
Title:     "No, Tom, it wouldn't. It would hang round the money. I know the ways of
Title:  ghosts, and so do you."
Title:  
Title:     Tom began to fear that Huck was right. Misgivings gathered in his mind.
Title:  But presently an idea occurred to him --
Title:  
Title:     "Lookyhere, Huck, what fools we're making of ourselves! Injun Joe's ghost
Title:  ain't a going to come around where there's a cross!"
Title:  
Title:     The point was well taken. It had its effect.
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, I didn't think of that. But that's so. It's luck for us, that cross
Title:  is. I reckon we'll climb down there and have a hunt for that box."
Title:  
Title:     Tom went first, cutting rude steps in the clay hill as he descended. Huck
Title:  followed. Four avenues opened out of the small cavern which the great rock
Title:  stood in. The boys examined three of them with no result. They found a small
Title:  recess in the one nearest the base of the rock, with a pallet of blankets
Title:  spread down in it; also an old suspender, some bacon rind, and the
Title:  well-gnawed bones of two or three fowls. But there was no money-box. The
Title:  lads searched and researched this place, but in vain. Tom said:
Title:  
Title:     "He said under the cross. Well, this comes nearest to being under the
Title:  cross. It can't be under the rock itself, because that sets solid on the
Title:  ground."
Title:  
Title:     They searched everywhere once more, and then sat down discouraged. Huck
Title:  could suggest nothing. By-and-by Tom said:
Title:  
Title:     "Lookyhere, Huck, there's footprints and some candle-grease on the clay
Title:  about one side of this rock, but not on the other sides. Now, what's that
Title:  for? I bet you the money is under the rock. I'm going to dig in the clay."
Title:  
Title:     "That ain't no bad notion, Tom!" said Huck with animation.
Title:  
Title:     Tom's "real Barlow" was out at once, and he had not dug four inches
Title:  before he struck wood.
Title:  
Title:     "Hey, Huck! -- you hear that?"
Title:  
Title:     Huck began to dig and scratch now. Some boards were soon uncovered and
Title:  removed. They had concealed a natural chasm which led under the rock. Tom
Title:  got into this and held his candle as far under the rock as he could, but
Title:  said he could not see to the end of the rift. He proposed to explore. He
Title:  stooped and passed under; the narrow way descended gradually. He followed
Title:  its winding course, first to the right, then to the left, Huck at his heels.
Title:  Tom turned a short curve, by-and-by, and exclaimed:
Title:  
Title:     "My goodness, Huck, lookyhere!"
Title:  
Title:     It was the treasure-box, sure enough, occupying a snug little cavern,
Title:  along with an empty powder-keg, a couple of guns in leather cases, two or
Title:  three pairs of old moccasins, a leather belt, and some other rubbish well
Title:  soaked with the water-drip.
Title:  
Title:     "Got it at last!" said Huck, ploughing among the tarnished coins with his
Title:  hand. "My, but we're rich, Tom!"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, I always reckoned we'd get it. It's just too good to believe, but
Title:  we have got it, sure! Say -- let's not fool around here. Let's snake it out.
Title:  Lemme see if I can lift the box."
Title:  
Title:     It weighed about fifty pounds. Tom could lift
Title:  it, after an awkward fashion, but could not carry it conveniently.
Title:  
Title:     "I thought so," he said; "they carried it like it was heavy, that day at
Title:  the ha'nted house. I noticed that. I reckon I was right to think of fetching
Title:  the little bags along."
Title:  
Title:     The money was soon in the bags and the boys took it up to the cross rock.
Title:  
Title:     "Now less fetch the guns and things," said Huck.
Title:  
Title:     "No, Huck -- leave them there. They're just the tricks to have when we go
Title:  to robbing. We'll keep them there all the time, and we'll hold our orgies
Title:  there, too. It's an awful snug place for orgies."
Title:  
Title:     "What orgies?"
Title:  
Title:     "I dono. But robbers always have orgies, and of course we've got to have
Title:  them, too. Come along, Huck, we've been in here a long time. It's getting
Title:  late, I reckon. I'm hungry, too. We'll eat and smoke when we get to the
Title:  skiff."
Title:  
Title:     They presently emerged into the clump of sumach bushes, looked warily
Title:  out, found the coast clear, and were soon lunching and smoking in the skiff.
Title:  As the sun dipped toward the horizon they pushed out and got under way. Tom
Title:  skimmed up the shore through the long twilight, chatting cheerily with Huck,
Title:  and landed shortly after dark.
Title:  
Title:     "Now, Huck," said Tom, "we'll hide the money in the loft of the widow's
Title:  woodshed, and I'll come
Title:  up in the morning and we'll count it and divide, and then we'll hunt up a
Title:  place out in the woods for it where it will be safe. Just you lay quiet here
Title:  and watch the stuff till I run and hook Benny Taylor's little wagon; I won't
Title:  be gone a minute."
Title:  
Title:     He disappeared, and presently returned with the wagon, put the two small
Title:  sacks into it, threw some old rags on top of them, and started off, dragging
Title:  his cargo behind him. When the boys reached the Welshman's house, they
Title:  stopped to rest. Just as they were about to move on, the Welshman stepped
Title:  out and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Hallo, who's that?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck and Tom Sawyer."
Title:  
Title:     "Good! Come along with me, boys, you are keeping everybody waiting. Here
Title:  -- hurry up, trot ahead -- I'll haul the wagon for you. Why, it's not as
Title:  light as it might be. Got bricks in it? -- or old metal?"
Title:  
Title:     "Old metal," said Tom.
Title:  
Title:     "I judged so; the boys in this town will take more trouble and fool away
Title:  more time hunting up six bits' worth of old iron to sell to the foundry than
Title:  they would to make twice the money at regular work. But that's human nature
Title:  -- hurry along, hurry along!"
Title:  
Title:     The boys wanted to know what the hurry was about.
Title:  
Title:     "Never mind; you'll see, when we get to the Widow Douglas'."
Title:  
Title:     Huck said with some apprehension -- for he was long used to being falsely
Title:  accused:
Title:  
Title:     "Mr. Jones, we haven't been doing nothing."
Title:  
Title:     The Welshman laughed.
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I don't know, Huck, my boy. I don't know about that. Ain't you and
Title:  the widow good friends?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes. Well, she's ben good friends to me, anyway."
Title:  
Title:     "All right, then. What do you want to be afraid for?"
Title:  
Title:     This question was not entirely answered in Huck's slow mind before he
Title:  found himself pushed, along with Tom, into Mrs. Douglas' drawing-room. Mr.
Title:  Jones left the wagon near the door and followed.
Title:  
Title:     The place was grandly lighted, and everybody that was of any consequence
Title:  in the village was there. The Thatchers were there, the Harpers, the
Title:  Rogerses, Aunt Polly, Sid, Mary, the minister, the editor, and a great many
Title:  more, and all dressed in their best. The widow received the boys as heartily
Title:  as any one could well receive two such looking beings. They were covered
Title:  with clay and candle-grease. Aunt Polly blushed crimson with humiliation,
Title:  and frowned and shook her head at Tom. Nobody suffered half as much as the
Title:  two boys did, however. Mr. Jones said:
Title:  
Title:     "Tom wasn't at home, yet, so I gave him up; but I stumbled on him and
Title:  Huck right at my door, and so I just brought them along in a hurry."
Title:  
Title:     "And you did just right," said the widow. "Come with me, boys."
Title:  
Title:     She took them to a bedchamber and said:
Title:  
Title:     "Now wash and dress yourselves. Here are two new suits of clothes --
Title:  shirts, socks, everything complete. They're Huck's -- no, no thanks, Huck --
Title:  Mr. Jones bought one and I the other. But they'll fit both of you. Get into
Title:  them. We'll wait -- come down when you are slicked up enough."
Title:  
Title:     Then she left.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXXIV
Title:  
Title:     HUCK said: "Tom, we can slope, if we can find a rope. The window ain't
Title:  high from the ground."
Title:  
Title:     "Shucks! what do you want to slope for?"
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I ain't used to that kind of a crowd. I can't stand it. I ain't
Title:  going down there, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, bother! It ain't anything. I don't mind it a bit. I'll take care of
Title:  you."
Title:  
Title:     Sid appeared.
Title:  
Title:     "Tom," said he, "auntie has been waiting for you all the afternoon. Mary
Title:  got your Sunday clothes ready, and everybody's been fretting about you. Say
Title:  -- ain't this grease and clay, on your clothes?"
Title:  
Title:     "Now, Mr. Siddy, you jist 'tend to your own business. What's all this
Title:  blow-out about, anyway?"
Title:  
Title:     "It's one of the widow's parties that she's always having. This time it's
Title:  for the Welshman and his sons, on account of that scrape they helped her out
Title:  of the other night. And say -- I can tell you something, if you want to
Title:  know."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, what?"
Title:  
Title:     "Why, old Mr. Jones is going to try to spring something on the people
Title:  here to-night, but I overheard him tell auntie to-day about it, as a secret,
Title:  but I reckon it's not much of a secret now. Everybody knows -- the widow,
Title:  too, for all she tries to let on she don't. Mr. Jones was bound Huck should
Title:  be here -- couldn't get along with his grand secret without Huck, you know!"
Title:  
Title:     "Secret about what, Sid?"
Title:  
Title:     "About Huck tracking the robbers to the widow's. I reckon Mr. Jones was
Title:  going to make a grand time over his surprise, but I bet you it will drop
Title:  pretty flat."
Title:  
Title:     Sid chuckled in a very contented and satisfied way.
Title:  
Title:     "Sid, was it you that told?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, never mind who it was. Somebody told -- that's enough."
Title:  
Title:     "Sid, there's only one person in this town mean enough to do that, and
Title:  that's you. If you had been in Huck's place you'd 'a' sneaked down the hill
Title:  and never told anybody on the robbers. You can't do any but mean things, and
Title:  you can't bear to see anybody praised for doing good ones. There -- no
Title:  thanks, as the widow says" -- and Tom cuffed Sid's ears and helped him to
Title:  the door with several kicks. "Now go and tell auntie if you dare -- and
Title:  to-morrow you'll catch it!"
Title:  
Title:     Some minutes later the widow's guests were at the supper-table, and a
Title:  dozen children were propped up
Title:  at little side-tables in the same room, after the fashion of that country
Title:  and that day. At the proper time Mr. Jones made his little speech, in which
Title:  he thanked the widow for the honor she was doing himself and his sons, but
Title:  said that there was another person whose modesty --
Title:  
Title:     And so forth and so on. He sprung his secret about Huck's share in the
Title:  adventure in the finest dramatic manner he was master of, but the surprise
Title:  it occasioned was largely counterfeit and not as clamorous and effusive as
Title:  it might have been under happier circumstances. However, the widow made a
Title:  pretty fair show of astonishment, and heaped so many compliments and so much
Title:  gratitude upon Huck that he almost forgot the nearly intolerable discomfort
Title:  of his new clothes in the entirely intolerable discomfort of being set up as
Title:  a target for everybody's gaze and everybody's laudations.
Title:  
Title:     The widow said she meant to give Huck a home under her roof and have him
Title:  educated; and that when she could spare the money she would start him in
Title:  business in a modest way. Tom's chance was come. He said:
Title:  
Title:     "Huck don't need it. Huck's rich."
Title:  
Title:     Nothing but a heavy strain upon the good manners of the company kept back
Title:  the due and proper complimentary laugh at this pleasant joke. But the
Title:  silence was a little awkward. Tom broke it:
Title:  
Title:     "Huck's got money. Maybe you don't believe it, but he's got lots of it.
Title:  Oh, you needn't smile
Title:  -- I reckon I can show you. You just wait a minute."
Title:  
Title:     Tom ran out of doors. The company looked at each other with a perplexed
Title:  interest -- and inquiringly at Huck, who was tongue-tied.
Title:  
Title:     "Sid, what ails Tom?" said Aunt Polly. "He -- well, there ain't ever any
Title:  making of that boy out. I never -- "
Title:  
Title:     Tom entered, struggling with the weight of his sacks, and Aunt Polly did
Title:  not finish her sentence. Tom poured the mass of yellow coin upon the table
Title:  and said:
Title:  
Title:     "There -- what did I tell you? Half of it's Huck's and half of it's
Title:  mine!"
Title:  
Title:     The spectacle took the general breath away. All gazed, nobody spoke for a
Title:  moment. Then there was a unanimous call for an explanation. Tom said he
Title:  could furnish it, and he did. The tale was long, but brimful of interest.
Title:  There was scarcely an interruption from any one to break the charm of its
Title:  flow. When he had finished, Mr. Jones said:
Title:  
Title:     "I thought I had fixed up a little surprise for this occasion, but it
Title:  don't amount to anything now. This one makes it sing mighty small, I'm
Title:  willing to allow."
Title:  
Title:     The money was counted. The sum amounted to a little over twelve thousand
Title:  dollars. It was more than any one present had ever seen at one time before,
Title:  though several persons were there who were worth considerably more than that
Title:  in property.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                  Chapter XXXV
Title:  
Title:     THE reader may rest satisfied that Tom's and Huck's windfall made a
Title:  mighty stir in the poor little village of St. Petersburg. So vast a sum, all
Title:  in actual cash, seemed next to incredible. It was talked about, gloated
Title:  over, glorified, until the reason of many of the citizens tottered under the
Title:  strain of the unhealthy excitement. Every "haunted" house in St. Petersburg
Title:  and the neighboring villages was dissected, plank by plank, and its
Title:  foundations dug up and ransacked for hidden treasure -- and not by boys, but
Title:  men -- pretty grave, unromantic men, too, some of them. Wherever Tom and
Title:  Huck appeared they were courted, admired, stared at. The boys were not able
Title:  to remember that their remarks had possessed weight before; but now their
Title:  sayings were treasured and repeated; everything they did seemed somehow to
Title:  be regarded as remarkable; they had evidently lost the power of doing and
Title:  saying commonplace things; moreover, their past history was raked up and
Title:  discovered to bear marks of conspicuous originality. The village paper
Title:  published biographical sketches of the boys.
Title:  
Title:     The Widow Douglas put Huck's money out at six per cent., and Judge
Title:  Thatcher did the same with Tom's at Aunt Polly's request. Each lad had an
Title:  income, now, that was simply prodigious -- a dollar for every week-day in
Title:  the year and half of the Sundays. It was just what the minister got -- no,
Title:  it was what he was promised -- he generally couldn't collect it. A dollar
Title:  and a quarter a week would board, lodge, and school a boy in those old
Title:  simple days -- and clothe him and wash him, too, for that matter.
Title:  
Title:     Judge Thatcher had conceived a great opinion of Tom. He said that no
Title:  commonplace boy would ever have got his daughter out of the cave. When Becky
Title:  told her father, in strict confidence, how Tom had taken her whipping at
Title:  school, the Judge was visibly moved; and when she pleaded grace for the
Title:  mighty lie which Tom had told in order to shift that whipping from her
Title:  shoulders to his own, the Judge said with a fine outburst that it was a
Title:  noble, a generous, a magnanimous lie -- a lie that was worthy to hold up its
Title:  head and march down through history breast to breast with George
Title:  Washington's lauded Truth about the hatchet! Becky thought her father had
Title:  never looked so tall and so superb as when he walked the floor and stamped
Title:  his foot and said that. She went straight off and told Tom about it.
Title:  
Title:     Judge Thatcher hoped to see Tom a great lawyer or a great soldier some
Title:  day. He said he meant to look to it that Tom should be admitted to the
Title:  National Military Academy and afterward trained in the best law school in
Title:  the country, in order that he might be ready for either career or both.
Title:  
Title:     Huck Finn's wealth and the fact that he was now under the Widow Douglas'
Title:  protection introduced him into society -- no, dragged him into it, hurled
Title:  him into it -- and his sufferings were almost more than he could bear. The
Title:  widow's servants kept him clean and neat, combed and brushed, and they
Title:  bedded him nightly in unsympathetic sheets that had not one little spot or
Title:  stain which he could press to his heart and know for a friend. He had to eat
Title:  with a knife and fork; he had to use napkin, cup, and plate; he had to learn
Title:  his book, he had to go to church; he had to talk so properly that speech was
Title:  become insipid in his mouth; whithersoever he turned, the bars and shackles
Title:  of civilization shut him in and bound him hand and foot.
Title:  
Title:     He bravely bore his miseries three weeks, and then one day turned up
Title:  missing. For forty-eight hours the widow hunted for him everywhere in great
Title:  distress. The public were profoundly concerned; they searched high and low,
Title:  they dragged the river for his body. Early the third morning Tom Sawyer
Title:  wisely went poking among some old empty hogsheads down behind the abandoned
Title:  slaughter-house, and in one of them he found the refugee. Huck had slept
Title:  there; he had just breakfasted upon some stolen odds and ends of food, and
Title:  was lying off, now, in comfort, with his pipe. He was unkempt,
Title:  uncombed, and clad in the same old ruin of rags that had made him
Title:  picturesque in the days when he was free and happy. Tom routed him out, told
Title:  him the trouble he had been causing, and urged him to go home. Huck's face
Title:  lost its tranquil content, and took a melancholy cast. He said:
Title:  
Title:     "Don't talk about it, Tom. I've tried it, and it don't work; it don't
Title:  work, Tom. It ain't for me; I ain't used to it. The widder's good to me, and
Title:  friendly; but I can't stand them ways. She makes me get up just at the same
Title:  time every morning; she makes me wash, they comb me all to thunder; she
Title:  won't let me sleep in the woodshed; I got to wear them blamed clothes that
Title:  just smothers me, Tom; they don't seem to any air git through 'em, somehow;
Title:  and they're so rotten nice that I can't set down, nor lay down, nor roll
Title:  around anywher's; I hain't slid on a cellar-door for -- well, it 'pears to
Title:  be years; I got to go to church and sweat and sweat -- I hate them ornery
Title:  sermons! I can't ketch a fly in there, I can't chaw. I got to wear shoes all
Title:  Sunday. The widder eats by a bell; she goes to bed by a bell; she gits up by
Title:  a bell -- everything's so awful reg'lar a body can't stand it."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, everybody does that way, Huck."
Title:  
Title:     "Tom, it don't make no difference. I ain't everybody, and I can't stand
Title:  it. It's awful to be tied up so. And grub comes too easy -- I don't take no
Title:  interest in vittles, that way. I got to ask to go a-fishing; I got to ask to
Title:  go in a-swimming --
Title:  dern'd if I hain't got to ask to do everything. Well, I'd got to talk so
Title:  nice it wasn't no comfort -- I'd got to go up in the attic and rip out
Title:  awhile, every day, to git a taste in my mouth, or I'd a died, Tom. The
Title:  widder wouldn't let me smoke; she wouldn't let me yell, she wouldn't let me
Title:  gape, nor stretch, nor scratch, before folks -- " [Then with a spasm of
Title:  special irritation and injury] -- "And dad fetch it, she prayed all the
Title:  time! I never see such a woman! I had to shove, Tom -- I just had to. And
Title:  besides, that school's going to open, and I'd a had to go to it -- well, I
Title:  wouldn't stand that, Tom. Looky-here, Tom, being rich ain't what it's
Title:  cracked up to be. It's just worry and worry, and sweat and sweat, and
Title:  a-wishing you was dead all the time. Now these clothes suits me, and this
Title:  bar'l suits me, and I ain't ever going to shake 'em any more. Tom, I
Title:  wouldn't ever got into all this trouble if it hadn't 'a' ben for that money;
Title:  now you just take my sheer of it along with your'n, and gimme a ten-center
Title:  sometimes -- not many times, becuz I don't give a dern for a thing 'thout
Title:  it's tollable hard to git -- and you go and beg off for me with the widder."
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, Huck, you know I can't do that. 'Tain't fair; and besides if you'll
Title:  try this thing just a while longer you'll come to like it."
Title:  
Title:     "Like it! Yes -- the way I'd like a hot stove if I was to set on it long
Title:  enough. No, Tom, I won't be rich, and I won't live in them cussed smothery
Title:  houses
Title:  . I like the woods, and the river, and hogsheads, and I'll stick to 'em,
Title:  too. Blame it all! just as we'd got guns, and a cave, and all just fixed to
Title:  rob, here this dern foolishness has got to come up and spile it all!"
Title:  
Title:     Tom saw his opportunity --
Title:  
Title:     "Lookyhere, Huck, being rich ain't going to keep me back from turning
Title:  robber."
Title:  
Title:     "No! Oh, good-licks; are you in real dead-wood earnest, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "Just as dead earnest as I'm sitting here. But Huck, we can't let you
Title:  into the gang if you ain't respectable, you know."
Title:  
Title:     Huck's joy was quenched.
Title:  
Title:     "Can't let me in, Tom? Didn't you let me go for a pirate?"
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, but that's different. A robber is more high-toned than what a
Title:  pirate is -- as a general thing. In most countries they're awful high up in
Title:  the nobility -- dukes and such."
Title:  
Title:     "Now, Tom, hain't you always ben friendly to me? You wouldn't shet me
Title:  out, would you, Tom? You wouldn't do that, now, would you, Tom?"
Title:  
Title:     "Huck, I wouldn't want to, and I don't want to -- but what would people
Title:  say? Why, they'd say, 'Mph! Tom Sawyer's Gang! pretty low characters in it!'
Title:  They'd mean you, Huck. You wouldn't like that, and I wouldn't."
Title:  
Title:     Huck was silent for some time, engaged in a mental struggle. Finally he
Title:  said:
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I'll go back to the widder for a month and tackle it and see if I
Title:  can come to stand it, if you'll let me b'long to the gang, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "All right, Huck, it's a whiz! Come along, old chap, and I'll ask the
Title:  widow to let up on you a little, Huck."
Title:  
Title:     "Will you, Tom -- now will you? That's good. If she'll let up on some of
Title:  the roughest things, I'll smoke private and cuss private, and crowd through
Title:  or bust. When you going to start the gang and turn robbers?"
Title:  
Title:     "Oh, right off. We'll get the boys together and have the initiation
Title:  to-night, maybe."
Title:  
Title:     "Have the which?"
Title:  
Title:     "Have the initiation."
Title:  
Title:     "What's that?"
Title:  
Title:     "It's to swear to stand by one another, and never tell the gang's
Title:  secrets, even if you're chopped all to flinders, and kill anybody and all
Title:  his family that hurts one of the gang."
Title:  
Title:     "That's gay -- that's mighty gay, Tom, I tell you."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, I bet it is. And all that swearing's got to be done at midnight,
Title:  in the lonesomest, awfulest place you can find -- a ha'nted house is the
Title:  best, but they're all ripped up now."
Title:  
Title:     "Well, midnight's good, anyway, Tom."
Title:  
Title:     "Yes, so it is. And you've got to swear on a coffin, and sign it with
Title:  blood."
Title:  
Title:     "Now, that's something like! Why, it's a
Title:  million times bullier than pirating. I'll stick to the widder till I rot,
Title:  Tom; and if I git to be a reg'lar ripper of a robber, and everybody talking
Title:  'bout it, I reckon she'll be proud she snaked me in out of the wet."
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:                                   CONCLUSION
Title:  
Title:     SO endeth this chronicle. It being strictly a history of a boy, it must
Title:  stop here; the story could not go much further without becoming the history
Title:  of a man. When one writes a novel about grown people, he knows exactly where
Title:  to stop -- that is, with a marriage; but when he writes of juveniles, he
Title:  must stop where he best can.
Title:  
Title:     Most of the characters that perform in this book still live, and are
Title:  prosperous and happy. Some day it may seem worth while to take up the story
Title:  of the younger ones again and see what sort of men and women they turned out
Title:  to be; therefore it will be wisest not to reveal any of that part of their
Title:  lives at present.
Title:  
Title:    ------------------------------------------------------------------------
